RATING: 8
Tentative rating after just one play - I instantly fell in love with this. The combination of Euro-style scoring mechanisms and deep theme were a hit in our first and only 2-player game.

Update: Played again, this time with three players. I feel like the amount of VP given for correctly identifying the killer is somewhat too high and tends to devalue the efforts made throughout the game - after all, there is a considerable amount of luck involved in placing the clues. But then again, the game itself, its theme and mechanics make it more than worthwile to spend hours on it...even if someone else wins to his own surprise because of one meagre clue point on his guy. I stand by my rating of 8.

RATING: 8.5
[thing=39339]Android[/thing]: 2-5 players (4+ hours).
The theme is excellent, with a beautiful board and with a lot of flavor text on the cards (you can decide yourself how much to read it during the game). All characters are unique with some of their own character-specific rules, personal storylines to finish during the game (happy endings give victory points), and their own demons to avoid when solving the case. There are many different murder cases to play. There is a great atmosphere, and although the board and the sheer number of tokens may at first intimidate you, the game mechanisms are actually quite simple when you have played once. Each game takes a long time (5+ hours for your first 2-player game), so stay clear of this game, if that is a problem for you.

I wish I could get this played more than once a year. :}

RATING: 3
I remember how excited I was when I heard about this game - how cool it looked. Then, I read reviews and decided that I was better off staying away. Well, a few weeks ago and years later, I happened to find a copy for $20 - what the heck? I though to myself - how bad can it be?

Well, its actually a pretty bad game. It wants to be thematic - but its really hard to get into the theme when there are so many things on the board. More than that, the game-play itself is very repetitive. It just didn't feel like... anything. 

If you want a story-game, get Tales of the Arabian Nights. That game may not have strategy but it can offer a great time. If you want a cyber-punk game, get Net Runner. 

Android is an academic curiosity at best. An example of how not to do theme in a game. As such, I enjoyed it - but don't get it if you're looking for a fun gaming experience.

RATING: 6
Amazingly detailed and complex world. Android is almost like playing 4 different small games simultaneously, and watching the ways in which they interlock is fascinating. There's the "whodunit" aspect, the personal development arc of your character, the AP system, the "conspiracy" puzzle (an ACTUAL PUZZLE built into the game board), and the ever-present time limit all coalesce to form a very unique experience.

The reasons I rated it lower than my praise may suggest:
1) The guilt/innocence of suspects is arbitrary. You're simply attempting to "frame" who you think is guilty. I think the game would be improved by actually having a guilty party, and adding dynamics for convicting them.
2) Complexity, rules-lawyering, and the time sink. This is a game on par with Twilight Imperium, Firefly, and Risk in terms of it's time commitment. I enjoy this game, but I rarely get it on the table for this reason. On top of the time sink, it takes a good 30 minutes (at least) to set up the game, and even longer to explain it to new players. Once you grok the rules, it's a fairly smooth game, but the initial learning curve is steep.
3) Bits. It's a FFG game, so you know there are seventy trillion small pieces. The board can get somewhat chaotic, and the footprint on this game is massive. We needed two tables to accommodate a 4 player game. 

If you want a hearty, deep experience requiring a good chunk of time to get lost in the narrative, get Android. The personal plots for each character are interesting touches and make players more invested. The mechanics work, and the art is fantastic. Just be aware of the learning curve and time requirements, and you'll have a great time.

RATING: 9
It is busy, and takes up a tonne of space, yet it is exceptional. The board itself is a blend of intertextual appropriation and functional iconography; add-in components and variously-sized cards with full art. It looks like a labour of love already. 

Mechanically, there's also a lot going on. However, I think for the fiddliness, the central mechanisms are brilliant and overcome the chrome. How the game asks you to frame a suspect inverts a lot of expectations of your typically inductive gamer. On top of this, the control the players have over narrative development through card play is sophisticated. Android draws you in. 


The dynamics of the role-playing detective mean I'd really want to play this with 3, maybe 2. There's too many chits lying around with more, but overall, it's about intimacy. This is a game where the world is bigger than the players - you don't 'win' in the conventional sense, but you will find a greater level of satisfaction in the result than most other games.

RATING: 3
In a way Android reminds me of another Kevin Wilson design, Arkham Horror.  It feels like in both chrome has been trowelled on to mask the fundamental simplicity of the game and make us believe it's worth several hours of our time.

In Android you are moving around the board picking up stuff, while playing helpful cards on yourself and unhelpful cards on others. Framing suspects (by picking up lead chits and drawing evidence chits), guiding personal stories to a happy conclusion (by picking up smiley chits) and collecting favours from two mega corporations at least offer some choice of focus. 

Framing suspects is the most painful element of the game.  Thanks to the random evidence chit draws, you can pay a lot of attention to it and come away with nothing.  Ignore it and watch someone else rack up a game winning score.  Wouldn't it be simpler to just flip a coin? 

Another element I dislike is the yawning chasms between turns.  However, you can't play a side game, read a book or make dinner because you need to be paying attention to your cards in case an opponent makes the play of one possible through their actions.  And another thing; only knowing how to give a player bad baggage by asking them is really poor.

The gravest sin of all for a game with so much chrome is that Android never comes to life thematically in the way that Arkham Horror does.  Its core, the murder mystery, is hollow, it never gets beyond move, pick up stuff, play cards.

RATING: 8
Cyberpunk + BoardGame + Anime art = Android
This is probably the best production fantasy flight has put out. From the box cover to the cards, board, counters, and rule book everything has been put together with attention to detail.
The rules are very clear and well ordered (seriously fantasy flight what's happened ;) ) The theme is piled on and the underlying game seems solid.
I'm this impressed even before playing it and that rarely happens! In fact I'm going to rate it now as a 9 without even playing... that how much I think I will like this.


Just played a two player to work out the rules and while fun it appears that the game could suffer some serious downtime issues in the learning stages. 
Also I think this has even more fiddly rules than Arkham Horror as each player has little exceptions here and there.
Rating lowered to an 8. I imagine it will stay around here.

Now have played 1/2 a three player game rating lowered again. This is disapointing.

Played a full 3 player game. It went much faster but still had major downtime issues. I think if more dark cards could drawn and held it would help this a bit. Still I'm going to persevre and play it a few more times as while some elements are horrible there are other that are really fun.

Played a 4 player.Took 5 hours for a full game so we're getting faster. Downtime STILL an issue, but players weren't drawing huge amounts of dark cards. Maybe reducing the time used each day and extending the number of weeks would improve things. I really want to like this game more :(

I've upped my rating to seven after a very enjoyable game that only took 4 hours including an explanation. Played light and fast this is a fun game. Let the mood wash over you and enjoy the ride. Vangelis in the background is a must.
Upped again to an 8. 3 hours ticked along nicely. Each time you play you peel back an extra layer of this game. Dark cards keep you in the game on other players turns and you must constantly be looking at your cards to work out what to do on your turn. 3 players is the sweet spot.

RATING: 7
This game has a reputation for being far more complicated then actual game play.  The basic mechanics are actually fairly easy.  Game play is highly thematic and if played as board game driven RPG can be quite fun.  The only odd or forced mechanism is the negative cards played against others.  There are times when the cards make perfect sense and fit the story and then there are times when it just feels mean to the opponent.  The game should be approached as a story game and an experience, not as a strategy game.  Play with folks of like mind and enjoy the experience.  Save the competitions for chess or something else.

RATING: 7
How can you possibly rate such a shambling behemoth after one play?  Not well is the answer.  It's longish (3 hours for 4 players + 45 minutes of rules).

Mechanics are strong.  "Take That" elements are well implemented and don't feel capricious.  A strong experience game with the potential to be tactically interesting as well.

Will require learning the characters to become a good player, but that may remove some of the experience.

Quibble.  The "story" is specific.  So rather than having generic events that you fill in with your own imagination and story, you have specific characters and specific actions, which I feel will ultimately become repetitive.  I personally would have preferred a more generic iconic approach to the cards rather than the specific world-building approach FFG took.

RATING: 8.6
Almost a perfect game. I rated 8.6 due to a bit of unnecessary complexity.

The atmosphere is brilliant. The stories are good, I'd like some more or different mechanics to convert favors into vps, and the thematic concept of a "favor" does not appeal well to me. I like the "arkham horror"-style board with the buildings of various uses and the personal character stories are a great addition to the game. The conspiracy puzzle provides some extra scoring conditions so it is also a useful element, although I disagree that you should be awarded vps for completing a line.

This games confuses me. I would like to rate it a 9. On the other hand it is probably the most confusing rules-wise game I've played. Some mechanics could be refined to offer the same gameplay with less complexity. And this comes from a person that LIKES complex games. Don't get me wrong: I like the fact that lots of things are going on in the game and I wouldn't like to see them reduced. However, in this level of complexity a game requires PERFECT mechanics. I rate the mechanics a 9/10 so overall the rating of this game drops from 9 to 8.6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Essentially Blade Runner: The Board Game. This is a weird game - as others have commented, it's like a Euro area-control game (investigation) hiding underneath a thick veneer of theme (moving around the board, personal stories, etc.).

The personal-story stuff works relatively well if you're getting into it - but the mechanism for it is counter-intuitive. The guilt/innocence part doesn't feel anything like an investigation - which isn't necessarily a bad thing, but does break the immersion in character quite a bit. The conspiracy part with the jigsaw is a nice idea, but thematically seems detached from the rest of the game and exists really just to score bonus points.

The main problem the game has is that - in keeping with some other Fantasy Flight games - it's a fiddly beast with a million pieces and it takes a long time to complete a game... which in itself kind of puts me off playing it. Which is a shame, because it's a really nice idea and so close to being brilliant.

RATING: 7.8
This is one of those games where the story told by the game is more important than the actual outcome of the game. Don't get me wrong, there is a pretty good game here with challenging and fullfilling gameplay. But the biggest part of the enjoyment comes from the narrative the players create while playing. It's too bad that this nice storytelling sometimes gets obscured by layers and layers of mechanics which do work gamewise but do not always help the narrative. There is a pretty big take that feel to the game. It's not as bad as some reviews may lead you to believe though. If you read the strategy guides provided for each character, you can avoid most of the bad stuff that can happen to you. If you're into theme and storytelling games this game could be one of your favorites. If you just like to play to optimize your score and beat the game system this game will almost certainly fall flat for you. I for one love this game and appreciate the ambition and love that went into it. Even if the game has its flaws.

RATING: 5.5
A very dynamic game that seems somewhat broken.  The large point swings (15-20 VP) from finding suspects guilty/innocent overshadow the rest of the game.  You can spend time (actions) to advance your character's storyline, to move and plant evidence on suspects, to build puzzle pieces that might provide direct VPs or VP-modifiers and to play cards that directly harm another player.  The balance of good actions and bad actions is well done; after playing a card that is really good for yourself, you'll need to play bad cards on opponents before you can play another good card on yourself.  The main problem with the game is that it's really tough to do well if you don't earn points with your secret suspects... and it's not easy to ensure that your suspect will finish the way that you want him/her to.  It seems dumb that the evidence planting is done in secret when you have almost zero incentive to play evidence (either positive or negative) on anyone other than your 2 secret suspects.  Collecting guaranteed points and largely ignoring the evidence-planting is a sure fire way to lose the game... so I don't think there are multiple paths to victory... I think you must stay involved in the evidence planting to win but it's all just a crapshoot of who gets the best numbers to plant at that point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Es un juego temático ambientado en el universo ciberpunk. Cada personaje lleva un detective que tratará de resolver un caso de asesinato. Siguiendo las pistas, podrá descubrir además una conspiración en la que estén implicadas entidades relevantes. Además, tendrá que ir "luchando" con sus propias historias.. cada personaje tiene sus propios problemas y tendrá que tomar decisiones al respecto.
En cada turno los detectives tienen una cantidad de puntos de tiempo que gastar en diversas acciones. Cada turno representa un día de juego. La partida dura dos semanas. Al final de cada semana, se resuelve una historia personal.  En cuanto al asesinato, durante la investigación, el detective puede "apostar" por alguno de los sospechosos, colocando sobre ellos "evidencias". Al final, el que mayor valor tenga (sumando el número del reverso de la evidencias) es el culpable. Cada detective tiene una carta de culpable e inocente. Recibirá puntos si resulta al final que su carta de culpable coincide con el culpable desvelado al final de la partida.

Está bastante entretenido. No es complicado de jugar.. las mecánicas son muy sencillas. El gran mérito del juego es el tema. Es un juego muy cinematográfico. Es como ver una peli. 

Se supone que es de 3-5, pero a dos, por lo que opina la comunidad funciona sin problema. Eso sí, son cuatro horitas de partida.

RATING: N/A
2-Player variants to try:  Quoted from forums

2p with the normal rules and it works just fine, but instead of number_of_player+1 suspects we have +2 (4 suspects). it's harder to find out the other's hunches and easier to bluff
Or

A classic way to play 2 players is for each player to play 2 characters and your final score is either the total for both or the lowest of the two.

If Raymond is in play, the other player should receive 2 memory cards, to balance the absence of the third player.

We keep drawing an event card until one turns up that applies to the current selection of NPCs or is in some way relevant to the game. Another way of looking at this is that we remove all events that apply to NPCs that are not in the game - but we are too lazy to actually sort through the deck and do so!

RATING: 5
Android is one of those games I want to love, but I just can't.  Yes, the narrative elements in this one are innovative - my problem is that the game underlying them is deadly dull, and not hearty enough to hold the weight of 2-3 hours of play.  I'm not one who is pissed off that the murder-mystery element becomes more about framing a suspect than detecting them - my issue is that this process is boring.  You basically have the main mechanic of the game being the manipulation of number values on a chart with 3 areas.  Other than doing that, you'll travel around and add a piece to the conspiracy "puzzle" every once in a while (also a dud in terms of fun), and you'll work on your personal issue subplots.  The idea of personal "baggage" and moving your character through choose-your-own-adventure style quests is the most interesting element in the game - but after a few plays, the novelty of this has been exhausted, and during those plays you want to focus on this element because it is the most fun, but it's not the focus of the game.  I think in all honesty what is at heart a Euro like this just can't hold the weight of all that narrative - most boardgames are not meant to (nor can they) tell compelling stories.  This one had promise, but at the end of the day I'd rather play a game like Arkham Horror.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The game that finally got me to start liking Netrunner. Though I had played the LCG over a dozen times, I never really understood or got into the theme. Once I played Android, I couldn't get enough: I pulled out my old Netrunner data packs to read the flavor text, and even began reading the Android novels to learn more about this cool futuristic world. 

The story element is unlike any I have ever seen in a board game. Your character has a backstory, their own friends and connections who occupy the board and can even die, and you get an entire plot line that changes from game to game. Your characters literally collect psychological baggage that affects their productivity and has a significant impact on the events of the game. The result is that the game really draws you in, and you actually start to get attached to your own character.  

The game is long, though, and mechanically, it's a bit of a mess: memory is a hugely important element, which I'm not a fan of, and most of your success in the game relies on lucky draws (evidence chits and character cards). Nevertheless, the game succeeds brilliantly in its creation of story and atmosphere, so much so that this is one of the only games I own where I don't really care who wins.

RATING: 4
Ultimately, it's one of those really long games where you can still win in the end by flipping a coin. 

There are some really neat things going on such as the car movement mechanic, the light/dark shifting mechanic for playing cards, the guilty/innocent  area influence hunches that are 'secret missions' and the neat plot progression.  However, these mechanics are cobbled together in a clunky manner and fail to produce much of a 'game'.  The tokens you collect (which may be too hard to acquire) make you think you need to have a long term strategy to succeed but the dark cards can destroy any hard fought for progress to the point that long term planning is essentially useless. You could spend the entire game focused on putting a hit on somebody and never get it accomplished because you keep getting screwed with.  For me, its' not satisfying to set out on an objective and never come close to achieving it.  In fact, it's downright frustrating and a reason why I won't play it again.

What the game essentially boils down to is running around the board chasing evidence and taking advantage of tactical point scoring oppurtunities. Long term planning is a recipe for failure and even if you put yourself in the best position to win, a lucky card/bag draw can swing the game by upwards of 20+ pts on the last turn.  As a side note, I feel I could really love this game if the dark cards were more of a deterrence/hinderance type of screw your neighbor and not so much of a "set you back 3 steps" destruction of process.  And dark cards that trigger on going to major locations are garbage.

RATING: 7
What a game!
I have playde this a few times now, and I'm still learning how to handle that beast. Its full of stories and atmosphere but first you have to dive through layers and layers of rules and mechanics. About one and a half hour for set up and explanation with new players. The game itself then takes about four hours.
Your gaming troupe needs to be commited to enjoy. Otherwise the experience is bound to be a frustrating one.
Play for the stories in your first rounds. Dont play for winning! It is hard to keep an eye on who is acctually doing best, thus in the end it often turns out that you where hindering the wrong detective.
The more often you play the more you will find strategies that enhance your winning chances.

RATING: 9
Tough to rate.  I absolutely love the theme.  I also really like that each character has a personal narrative to explore while there is simultaneously a global narrative that unfolds.  It really brings the characters alive and creates an attachment for me similar to role-playing games.

However, I don't like the action point system.  That can lead AP prone players to take a *long* time on their turns, which means Android will drag out for quite a while.  I'm also not sure about the Twilight card help/sham mechanism.  In principle it's fine, but because you have sham cards, you have to pay close attention while the other players take their long drawn out turns.  I would almost rather not be engaged during another player's turn.

In the end, though, I just like the theme and the cardplay enough that they overcome the other issues.  I feel like this could be a template for a similar game which is maybe a bit more streamlined.  I've seen it described as a multi-player fantasy card driven wargame, and that's not too far off.  To play well, you would need to understand your character's specific deck.  I'm hoping to play it quite a few more times, which says something given how long it is.  If it pans out for two players, then it will see even more play.

RATING: 5
Oh god, how much I want to love this game but can't.
This is a mixture of great mechanics what at the and fails to being a good game.

+:
*Light-dark twilight cards mechanic forces you to use both because of the light-dark shifting. Great idea to motivate every player to play both. This causes  a good amount of confrontation.
*The plots. Such an elegant way to tell a story to every character which can be very different in game after game AND have an effect on end game scoring too.
*You are building a puzzle during the game (conspiracy part)

-:
*Overwhelming. You have too many options. I don't think any of them is bad but this game is like you add 3-4 expansions to the base game together.
*Way too long. One of the longest game what I ever played. Usually I have no problem with that but my group does.
*Downtime. You can sit there for 10-15 mins easily without anything to do. Especially with 5 players.
*Too many tokens, components etc. This makes setup a long process and hard to store the game. Also requires a lot of table space.

Overall I feel this game has some excellent ideas what should be implemented in other games too. On the other hand if the designer wouldn't want to put everything into this single game could end a better game.

RATING: 6

There is a lot to take in on the first play. That is not to say that the game is overly complicated, but there is a lot of different subsystems to learn. I am looking forward to trying this again.

If you are into short little euros with pasted on themes then don't even bother. If you like like long theme laden games then this might be the one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A very unique game with a myriad of components and way to score victory points. It has a LONG playtime (we took 5 hours for a 5 player game, all but one of us noobs), but if I am playing a 5 player game, I'd rather play this over other FFG games, say......... Arkham Horror.

You are a character in a dystopian future earth, trying to investigate a crime. There are 4 main ways to score VPs: (1) trying to incriminate the suspects you think are guilty and protecting those who you think are innocent; (2) progressing your character's personal storyline; (3) discovering parts of 'The Conspiracy'; (4) gaining favors and spending them to get in the good favors of the corporations.

A very unique game, and if you are in the longer games crowd, a winner.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Only played one incomplete game. Everyone was intrigued, but the lateness of the hour combined with the game's enormous scope and detail made it a poor choice for that particular night. This will need a few more plays before I can formulate an accurate rating.

Update: Finally got this to the table again for a 3-player game. My first full game; the others had played before, but not for some time. A little over four hours from start to finish; there was a lot of brushing up on rules in the first 90 minutes, but then everyone was up to speed. It. Is. Amazing. Yes, it's a collection of many discrete elements (mechanics), but somehow they all work together to create a unique game experience. I guess it's a bit like Karloff's monster in "Frankenstein"... he may be stitched together from many different sources, but he's his own unique individual. I can't wait to play this again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Interesting game. You move your investigator around the map and collect favors and evidence as you build your case. The catch is everyone else is doing the same thing, so you don't know if your hunches will be correct, which is a big deal given their VP value. You can mitigate it somewhat by talking to a couple of NPC's.

Meanwhile, your investigators have their own issues (plots) that are resolved as the game progresses, and these are also worth VP, and can be negatively affected by your opponents through the play of "dark" cards. Finally, there's the conspiracy board, which is a bit wonky, but can also be worth big VP points.

The whole thing is very thematic and the universe is pretty cool. I'm convinced that reading the flavor text and getting into the setting is crucial to enjoyment of the game. If nothing else it gives you something to do during downtime.

There's also a fan created "director's cut" set of variants that may appeal to some folks.

Update: After more play I've had to accept the fact, like so many others, that I like [i]idea[/i] of this game more than the game itself. For all its theme, the mechanics aren't that evocative given the density of the rules and result in too much down time with 6+ actions per turn, especially if someone at the table suffers from AP (and someone always does). Plus, the effects of the dark cards are too wild at times, with some cards nearly useless while others are brutal hosers. I do think that the Director's Cut variants address some of the issues regarding the disconnect between mechanics and theme, but come at the expense of adding more complexity to an already overworked set of rules.

RATING: 8
Quite simply, there is no better "story in a box" game than Android.  If you approach a game of Android as a VP-hunter, you've already lost the game; this is narrative-driven gameplay with victory points only used as a measuring stick of success at the end.  This is an epic game that will take the five wildly different characters on different journeys, all in the pursuit of their own goals and schemes.  It's a time investment to be sure, but this game takes the concept of "winning" and turns it on its ear in a satisfying way that never fails to be memorable.  If you value narrative in your games, and you're not afraid of a time commitment, this is one of the best.

RATING: N/A
This one is a keeper. It has one of the most interesting gameplays. 
However, NEVER play it with less than 5 players. These two leads missing make a huge difference. The same is true for the one character missing, as he would otherwise pick up (and thereby move) leads. We played with 4 players, and felt the drag of having to move further and further.
Also, NEVER play "Revenge at the Races". The Dark-shifting will slow down cardplay, leading to a less interesting game.
I'd also strongly advise thinking about playing "Mons Wolff Massacre" (increase in VP for Guilty hunches can wildly swing the game, probably out of balance) and "Last Call at Roxie's" (the possibility to kill more easily not only makes the game more frustrating, it can catapult players out of the game very easily).
The lead movement in "Murder on the Midway" could also swing the game, but I'm not sure if this would be felt harshly. And I don't see many problems in "Freetown Fright". So I would recommend playing these, or just the standard scenario, every time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Good, I think.  This game is like that one ex-girlfriend who was beautiful and passionate, but also a complete mess who just couldn't seem to keep it together.

Alternately, this game is a stunning labor of love, but with too much love and not enough labor.

Anyway, I'm out of cute metaphors.  Overall this is phenomenally hard to rate.  Some parts are a 10, some parts are a 4.  Clearly a game that rewards intense, repeat plays; but also so limited in its appeal (sci-fi noir, long playtime, complex rules) that it's hard to see how it will get the plays it deserves to really shine.

One response to a bogus criticism:  This game is NOT about "solving the mystery" in the same sense as [i]Clue[/i] or [i]Mystery of the Abbey[/i].  There are no absolutely true facts of a crime to uncover.  So people who complain that placing evidence on suspects isn't "thematic" are missing the point of the game.  You can view this two ways:

(1) Your character is actively trying to frame a suspect.  It's a dark future, get over it.

(2) My preferred way: Things that you (the player) do are not necessarily things that your character does.  You are guiding your character through a story; the unstated assumption/insistence that there must be some kind of one-to-one mapping of your actions to your character's actions is simply not true.

RATING: 3
Well, I thought I would try this since I have new found love for FFG.  In the end I didn't enjoy the game but I suspect that I should have known.  I don't feel burned by it and I was glad to have tried it, but in the end it's rated so low for two reasons: 

(1) Our first session was about 3 1/2 hours which is small compared to what others have reported.  So, the  game is long but it also feels really long due to the large amount of downtime in the game.  The worst is you have to pay attention during people's turns otherwise you may miss the opportunity to play a dark card on someone.  I don't mind the 'take that' elements of the game but when you lose your whole turn then the downtime becomes even worse again.

(2) I felt that a lot of the areas on the board were not worth the time investment compared to simply going and following a lead.  This could definitely be my own inexperience talking but spending 4 favors (each favor is technically worth about 2-3 time) plus extra time to put one hit token on a suspect just doesn't seem worthwhile.  Now I know you probably won't use this at all unless you're the character that can get these through light cards but I just didn't feel the balance.

Overall I liked that each character plays completely differently and you will even have some variation from game to game with each one.  It also has a great story going on and a lot of interlocking game mechanisms, some of which are new and innovative.  In the end, I likely won't play this game again due to the downtime but at least I got to experience it once.  I'm also grateful that it was the inspiration for personal stories in the Arkham Horror expansion Innsmouth Horror.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[1 play] Interesting game, with the scope, complexity, and fiddliness of Arkham Horror or Battlestar Galactica.  Our one play had 3-4 new people taught by someone who was on his 3rd play, so very much a learning game.  This is the sort of game that has a significant learning curve, so that you can't really get to whatever it has to offer unless you have a core of people interested enough to be willing to suffer some front-end pain.  It took us an hour of rules and then just over 5 hours to play.  

The alleged deductive element was one of the things that attracted me, but there wasn't that much of one.  There's a murder-mystery theme, but you don't so much solve the murder (by figuring out who actually IS guilty) as try to make whoever you want be guilty.  The deduction probably enters in more in terms of figuring out what your fellow players are trying to do and what their secret goals are, and that was a level of subtlety we were not prepared to cope with on the first time out.  It really seems like at first you don't have the knowledge to play the game very strategically.

Another problem with this kind of game is that one of the strengths of the game is its theme-richness and immersiveness; however, when you're blundering through it for the first time and it's taking forever, one of the first things you're inclined to sacrifice is reading the flavor text and so forth.  But without that, you lose something that makes the game enjoyable.

Probably the most interesting aspect of the game to me was my individual character and her plots.  Each character has a set of possible subplots about their personal life story that you try to make end favorably.  If I had it to do over again, what would have been most helpful to me would have been, before the game, to read all the character sheets, for a better understanding of who everyone was and how they interacted.  It's also useful to see what advice the other characters are given about how to combat your character!

I would probably play this again.  I definitely would if I had the chance to either play with people who knew what they were doing (though the newer player would likely get destroyed in that case), or play with people who were committed to playing a few times.  This game could be good if you got past the initial learning curve--how good, unfortunately, I'm not likely to find out.

RATING: 10
Not a mystery solving game. The goal of this game is to get invested in your character and help him/her through the game as best as possible. 
There is a lot of tension when you have to decide between resolving your plot positively vs going for more points somewhere else. Just like in real live you can't succeed at everything. This is really an rpg game.

I always play with our house rules that allow for all the players to take their turns simultaneously. Feels like there is more of a time crunch make sure you keep those dark cards in mind. This same rule also makes those dark cards seem less like a personal attack played by a bunch of hungry vulture players and more like a bad card in a game of munchkin just a plot complication that adds fun. Try it out!

RATING: 7
This game is too long. That's its fault.
But I do think that played with the right group, and having the right amount of time at your disposal, it makes a great experience.
You need to:
- enjoy the theme
- take time to get into the setting (read the stories of the detectives beforehand)
- have enough time
- read the cards aloud
- don't play to win.

You will rarely play another game like this.
It's not perfect, but it's unique.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Just played it for the first time, and I expected to enjoy it much more than I did. I generally don't have a problem with games that have a long set-up time and many small pieces as long as all those pieces and all that setup contribute to the theme in very tangible ways.

It's such a shame that a promising game like this struggles in so many ways. The attempt to blend luck and tactical play only manages to be frustrating, although I don't know if the deck can really be considered a luck element with so many repeat trash cards. I was very excited about my character when the game started, but after cycling through half my deck, I found her abilities tedious at best. I had already deduced the other player's hunches, and yet I was stuck with 3 cards that allowed me to look at the evidence tiles on one suspect.

The board is also irritating. At first I found all the names charming, as well as the idea that various locations were primarily corporate, political, religious, etc. However, the filler-spaces in combination with the finicky and time consuming movement restriction made me begin to dread my oncoming turns if only because I had to use that stupid half-moon movement piece. The only satisfying use for that piece would be poking other players who take too long on their turns. For that purpose it excels, because it is quite sharp.

I'm sure I'm not the only person who expected this game to feel like an interactive Bladerunner. Unfortunately, this was not the case. Somewhere in between moving your Time track, adjusting the Day track, keeping up with all 4 Favor token types, all 3 Lead token types, your own Light-Dark track, various event cards and the deadening, sunken, reeking mire that is the Light and Dark cards, it became a chore to read the flavor text. When we did bother to do this, the descriptions were repetitive and frankly boring. Oh, the poor Bioroid is struggling with hate crimes and his own newborn humanity? Oh no, look! The Psychic Clone has a crush, but her helicopter boss is making her sad. Allow me to wipe away my single tear.

The common defense of this game is that the theme should not be the murder you're investigating, but instead the characters each person is playing. If that is the case, why does the game rely so much upon highly abstracted, Euro-esque mechanics? Euros with such clunky mechanics would be an abject failure at its core, and while I can understand clunky mechanics that add accuracy to an Ameritrash theme, so many of Android's mechanics are not. Why is it necessary to have 4 types of influence, plus the occasional Wild? It ceases to effectively add theme when the players want to strangle themselves.

I really do want to like this game. I intend to try it again with 3 players and see if that makes a difference, because with a theme so promising, I would be welcome to the idea that my initial judgement was unfair.

RATING: 6
The setting for this world is a lot like Richard K. Morgan's Takeshi Kovacs novels - a mixture of Philip K. Dick and Dashiell Hammett.

Pro: The three separate divisions of the game - the murder case, the conspiracy, and the personal lives of the detective characters are superbly done. The light/dark mechanic, the puzzle pieces, the car rulers, and the mechanic for setting clue locations are all interesting. The theme and artwork are  excellent 

Con: The game is very reading intensive and the text on the cards is small. This is one game where the flavor text really matters and the small italic text is difficult for my 50 year old eyes to read. This is really the only major negative I see to the game but its a big one. This cost it at least 2 points in my rating. The sheer amount of color on the board can render the evidence markers hard to see. I believe this would make it difficult for a color-blind individual to play.

The bottom line: I enjoyed the game and would like to play again but I will need advance warning so I can bring a magnifying device to read and enjoy the text parts of the game.

RATING: 10
This game has a terrifically well thought out cyberpunk world with just the right tone of film noir and science fictional extrapolations. Theme (or fluff as some may call it) is integral to the game's underlying mechanics and is not just there to draw in those of us that like the near future setting. Fantasy adventure boardgames may take us on the Campbellian journey of the hero, but this is the closest I've felt to really playing my character in a boardgame. Yet, the game also has some very strong strategic elements (action points, hand management, area control, etc). One warning, if you are looking for Clue(do) or Mystery in the Abbey, look somewhere else, this is not a deduction game in the traditional sense.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Probably the most fascinating and clever game I played. If only it was shorter... There are three ways to obtain points, determining the final winner:
1) A murder has happened: every character is a detective, competing to solve the case (although mechanically you are seemly more framing the murderer than finding him): during the game you pick up clues valued from -3 to 5 and add them to one suspect. In the end, the most guilty is THE murderer and you gain points if it was your "guilty hunch". Viceversa, you gain (less) points if your "innocent hunch" is NOT the murderer
2) A conspiracy is going on. You "uncover" it (mechanically you connect pieces of a jigsaw puzzle connecting different corporations with the crime) and gain points directly and indirectly (connected corps give some tokens of yours additional value).
3) Your personal story enters into game, with bad and good events determined by cards and other factors. There are checkpoints every 3 turns in which you count the "baggage" and follow the story towards greatness or failure.

Around this, nice and flavourful mechanics (like the cars represented by a measurer) and a system of good (for you) and bad cards (for opponents) which prevents you from "turtling" and ignoring other players.

Indeed a piece of art.
Rarely played (takes easily >4 hours), but I will never trade it away (unless a new version comes out, that's it)

RATING: 5
I've only played once so far, and that was with 5 players -- a BIG mistake.  While I admire the creativity and originality, I found the game to be:
 
1) WAY too long.  We played with 5 players -- 3 new to the game -- and it took us right at 6 hours to play.  Yes, we talked and joked, but we do that in every game we play.  I would NOT play the game again with 5 players.  
 
2) There is WAY too much going on -- it is confusing.  This is coming from a guy who grew-up in the hobby playing complex war games that often had rule books 60 or more pages long.  I'm familiar with complex rules.  This one was just plain confusing.  In an admirable effort to develop a game that really evokes the complex theme, the folks at Fantasy Flight have given us a game that is on the edge of being too complex and confusing.  Indeed, for me, it may have jumped off that edge.
 
3) Story.  I also used to be a role-player, so I love immersing myself in a good story.  I never felt I was actually living the story.  We all did our best by reading aloud all of the backgrounds, plots and flavor text.  For me, it just didn't work.  The game failed to bring the story to life for me.  While some rave about the mechanism of placing evidence on the suspects in order to determine the ultimate perpetrator, I found this aspect to be weak and quite random.  This is NOT a detective game.  Rather, as one of my fellow East Tennessee Gamers put it, players assume the role of producers trying to lead their script to the best conclusion.  
 
There is no denying that the game has some very creative elements and innovations.  I really liked the puzzle-like conspiracy aspect.  Sadly, with five players, this played out before we were finished with the first half of the game.  With less players it would likely last longer, which would be better.  We also depleted the evidence tokens with several turns remaining in the game, greatly restricting our options over the final few turns of the game.
 
There is much to like here, especially for fans of American-style games.  For me, it was a matter of theme over substance, and it just took WAY too long and was WAY too confusing.  The confusing aspect would likely diminish with multiple playings, but it is not a game that I would play often due to its length.  Yes, the old argument of the playing time being reduced with subesequent playings could apply, but that assumes the game will be played with all experienced players in each playing.  That rarely happens in our group, as we always have folks new to a game involved in just about every playing.  Thus, I don't see the time to play being reduced substantially.
 
Will I play again?  Maybe -- but only with 3 or 4 players.  I will NOT devote a full day to this game -- it isn't that good.

RATING: 8
Not for the fainted heart... This is a niche game.

Need to approach this game with the proper mindset, which is IMO: "Let's hang out in this noir universe and explore the poor destinies of some broken guys against overhelming forces".

Have fun, take your time, expriment and relax and you'll be rewarded. Avoid this game if you are looking for elegant mechanisms and optimization play. It will be too dry for your taste. It's all about the universe, not about the winner.

3 players is the sweet spot. 6 hours for a first game. 3-4 hours for the following.

RATING: 6
How can a game that looks so great play so lifeless?  I didn't like the frame-the-guilty system.  Seemed completely backward from how it should work.  You could have had a really immersive clue/deduction system but instead we find ourselves placing numbered tiles in boxes to be counted up later.  The conspiracy puzzle was a total abstraction that can result in some strong combos.  I really liked the plot system and thought it was well done.  The movement and resource management actions wewre pretty cool but I think the game could have been made with about 1/3 of the tokens they included.  Its also pretty long and repetitive as you run around doing basically the same thing every turn, then the game climaxes with a massive VP counting session.

RATING: 6
Dear God. This game suffers from a serious case of "bit inflation." It's like the designer couldn't bear to eliminate any bit of chrome, and the publishers felt the need to create enough tokens to use up some vast underground hoard of cardboard. Entire forrests must have been wiped out for this. There are good mechanics in here, but they're buried under too much fiddly detail (warrants, hits, excessive victory point mechanisms, maybe even the conspiracy angle). There is so much to learn that just explaining the rules will take up most of an afternoon, and then so much to do that gameplay could keep you going through nuclear winter. I'm willing to play it some more because I like the theme and some of the mechanics are very good indeed (like the plots, which on first reading the rules I thought were completely extraneous to the game) and the cool movement and evidence systems. But finally, why is it named "Android?" It has nothing to do with androids...

RATING: 10
[i][b]D[/b]isturbed, sci-fi noir detectives have two weeks to frame a perp for murder while chainsaw-juggling their own personal demons.  Someone gets to keep their job, and maybe their sanity—will it be you?[/i]

[q="MScrivner"]Android is not really a game - it's a delivery system for a work of literature.[/q]
Like playing Poker with Burrough's [url=http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cut-up_technique]cut-ups[/url].

[microbadge=7155]

[i]Seedy, sleazy, brilliant.[/i]

A dizzyingly ambitious design, a story engine with a mechanical core that has so many wheels within wheels that the conspiracy you're manufacturing has to be real... right?

I imagine this will hit a 10 for me, but repeat plays will tell.

The good news is I'll have no trouble getting this to the table for more.

UPDATE:  Why wait?  It's a 10.  How can I say that?  Because [thread=364669][i]Android[/i] haunts me[/thread], that's why.

[microbadge=7811]

[b]FOR YOUR FIRST GAME:[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]• Set aside a five-hour block a week in advance.[/COLOR]
This is not a pick-up game—it's an EVENT.

[COLOR=#FF0000]• Have all players read the rules ahead of time.[/COLOR]
If they're unwilling to do this they're not up for the experience.

[COLOR=#FF0000]• 3 players ONLY.[/COLOR]
Five is just waaay too many.  Four is long and downtime can be brutal.  I'll only play this with three from now on.


[b]FOR SUBSEQUENT GAMES WITH NEW PLAYERS:[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]• Have them read the rules in advance.[/COLOR]
Explaining the rules [i]as part of the session[/i] can kill the momentum and turn all the fun into a gooey gray mess...

RATING: 7
I finally got a chance to play this over the weekend.  I really enjoyed it.  It is a very long game (especially since we had a full compliment of 5 players), but, the first 90 minutes were taken up by rules explanation (we still missed a few, but it ironed itself out ok during the game) and there were also meal and toilet/cigarette breaks.  You HAVE to play this with a group that will read, and enjoy, the flavor text.  Definitely a unique game and not like anything else out there (that I have played, anyway).  The last day/round got brutal as everyone began dumping all the bad cards they could on everyone else.  I wound up losing, but, had I placed a piece of evidence differently on the 3rd day/turn, I would have changed the guilty suspect and won...  However, I was the only player (as I recall) with a Happy Ending... and yes, my group made ALL the expected "happy ending" jokes throughout the game.  I was very impressed with Android overall.

RATING: 9
So let me start by saying that the folks giving it a low rating because it is complex and not like Net Runner need to research games before they buy them. 

Android is a masterpiece in every shape and form. From the quality of the box and art to the component quality. There is a ton of game in this box.  

The story flavor is immersive and does not let down in my opinion. This game is unconventional in a lot of ways.  From the beginning you are pinning a murder on a suspect, as well as the other player's so you may be going after a different suspect than everyone else. 

The balancing act of pinning the murder and trying to keep you're character in in good standing in their personal life is unusual and semi finicky but is what makes the game in my opinion. 

So if you are a fan Of Kevin Wilson's games, and like detailed games bordering an RPG with the incredible Android backdrop please do not hesitate to try this game. I do agree that the game is very complex and will not be for everyone, for someone like me who enjoys Arkham horror over Eldritch Horror or Descent 1st edition over 2nd edition Android does not disappoint in the slightest.

RATING: 4
There are some cute ideas in this game, e.g. the way movement works keeps the feel of tabletop ruler movement, but doesn't suffer from the arguments over whether something is just too far or not. However, the game is too fiddly-detail for me. You have to keep track of of little details in other players play areas, e..g. which plot they are following, whether their NPCs are alive, etc, which means if you were to try to play properly the game would grind to a halt. Also, the way evidence works seems very random. And there are some other irritations, e.g. in many places favours (red, green, yellow or blue tokens) are referred to only by name ("political", "street", etc), not by colour.

RATING: 9.8
Absolutely love this game. It's more like a noir film or hardboiled novel (with cyberpunk trappings) than it is a cyberpunk detective game. It focuses more on the five characters, their stories and world building than it does detective work (which, if you're at all familiar with film noir, isn't surprising). The mechanics mesh well, especially considering there are essentially several interlocking minigames that form the whole. 

It takes a really long time to set up and play, and is very confrontational in some ways, so that can turn some people off. And lots of folks don't like it because it's not a true deduction game, per se (again, it's based on film noir...where finding out who the identity of the killer is usually the last priority). I think it's an insanely rich, incredibly rewarding game.

RATING: 5
2016-07-24

Initial Rating: 5.0 (July 2016)

I was a bit troubled about playing Android with 5 players, but I relented. I knew, in all probability, that the game (being a FGG game) would have lots of downtime and I was right. The downtime is WAY TOO MUCH and that decreased the fun of the game a lot!

Android is a thematic game and the first one in the Android Universe (obviously :P). I love the theme and, as I love Android: Netrunner, I liked very much to see the thematic tie ins between both games. I really like the dystopian future in which megacorporations control the world. In Android, there is a murder you need to solve and you play as a detective who tries to solve. It drips theme everywhere, the flavor text is excellent.

The game comes with full bio for the characters adding to the theme setting (I love that) but also comes with lots of tips on how to play them, I somewhat dislike this because I play boardgames to discover the strategies for myself. For example, in my detective sheet (Rachel Beckman) says that I never should have less than 3 cards and at the end of a turn to avoid attacks. At the end of the game other players told me they never attacked me because they couldn’t as I always had more than 3 cards. I never had the opportunity to discover this by myself and that bothers me. It also said Rachel Beckman should never focus on her suspects and on the conspiracy (but my suspect was the guilty one at the end :D). In general I dislike when the rulebooks or the aids say how you should play the game. 

Explaining the game rules isn’t easy. The rules aren’t complex, but there are many of them. You can explain them under 30 minutes. Playtime is around 4 hours (or more), the 180 minutes advertised by the publisher is a gross underestimation. 

We played Android with 5 players and the downtime was excessive, sometimes it went for more than 15 minutes between turns, very boring. Some of the “take that” cards can reduce the amount of time you get each turn or even make you skip your whole turn, those card should be banned from the game as they aren’t fun in any way (although thy are certainly thematic). In our game, that situation happened to one player and he got asleep between turn because so much time passed until he got his turn back. The downtime is HUGE negative for me and the worst sin a boardgame can commit IMO. Due to this, I think Android is best with 3 players. I refuse to play it again with more players.

The game´s components are very good as is usual from FFG. The board´s art is great as is the art on the cards. I particularly liked the cool looking “car ruler” you use to move in the world. The conspiracy puzzle is also very well done!

In your turn, you get an allowance of time that you spend in various actions. The game decisions are very interesting (where to move, which kind of clue to follow, what suspect to covertly frame/vindicate, should I advance the conspiracy? Do I try to achieve my personal goal? Which kind of card to draw and from what player?, should I try to use an space action? What player should I attack and with which card? How to manage the light/dual aspect of the game? Which card to play?) and non-obvious.

The suspect system is great and it can give a lot of VPs at the end. While you play you can bluff, deduce who the suspects of you opponents are and manipulate them into helping you. I love this part of the game. Less great is the uncontrollable luck inherent in the evidence tokens. A player can take 4 pieces of evidence and still be overwhelmed by ONE piece of evidence from another detective, besides each suspect has a different ratio for different pieces of evidence, that is, the evidences are more or less valuable if you play them in different suspects. Once you are pretty sure who are the suspects of your opponents you can plot to kill them :devil:, I like that part as well. The best strategy here is to never allow anyone to know who are your suspects and that’s easier said than done, but it is possible. This is the best part of the game for me, I like maneuvering evidence to make my guys or the opponent’s more guilty/innocent as needed.

Android has a huge replayability factor as is also usual from FFG. Each game plays very different because you use a different scenario, each detective plays very differently and the personal goals are also different and appear in different order. The guilty/innocent suspects are also randomly dealt

There is a lot of uncontrollable luck with the game, the light/dark cards, the consequences of the attacks (you choose blindly many times), the evidence tokens, the random events (in my game an event happened very near where I was so I was the only player to get VPS from that location). Perhaps luck can be decreased if you know more about the cards, but I won’t play enough times to reach that threshold. All of this is a negative for me as the game takes a long time to play. You ability to plan ahead is very limited.

Bottom line, Android is an unique game with an epic feel to it. I would have loved it when I was a newbie in the hobby, but now, I have drifted away from most thematic games (I prefer superior gameplay and mechanisms even if they don´t make thematic sense). It has a VERY HIGH downtime and that’s the worst affliction a boardgame can have in my book. I also have drifted away from “take that“ games and the attack cards can be devastating in Android, furthermore if they take time from you as I previously wrote. I’m open to try it again with 3 players, but I’m selling my copy of the game ASAP. Desire to play it again is low. Android reminds me a bit of Arkham Horror (which I love, although I confess that’s due to nostalgia as it was one of the first boardgames I bought).

Current Rating: 5.0

RATING: 8.5
I love this game.  It is like no other board game out there today.  It is a "Story-driven" game & is not bogging players down in any "dice chucking" mechanics--with everything based on luck.  There's not just a murder mystery to solve but a conspiracy to reveal & an emphasis on managing your character's storyline within the game.  So much value is placed on whether your character ends their personal story at endgame on the upbeat otherwise it costs you in victory points.  I recommend this game to die-hard board gamers who are looking for something very different in their boardgaming experience.  It is a long & involved game so if you're looking for something "light", avoid this game.  You won't find it here.

RATING: 7
There are few games as polarizing as Android. After one play I can't tell which side I'm leaning to. I enjoyed it although I can see why people dislike it. 
We played with four and it took us more than five hours. It can happen that you have to wait 10 minutes until it's your turn again. There's an enourmous upfront investment in learning the game and its many subsystems. Much time is spent on managing the complexity of the game and decisions with doubtful relevance for the final outcome as luck of the draw and (in-)attentiveness of other players in (not) targetting somebody will have at least as much influence. Android clearly lives from the thematic experience.
For me, it's the competitive alternative to Arkham Horror. There's a potential that the take-that component tanks it. If the final determination of the murderer is as decisive and difficult to control as it seems it might reduce my enjoyment of the game.

RATING: 3
Played in bleached_lizard's heavily modified version. I'm sure it's a vast improvement over the original, but it's still a random game at heart, and there's no papering over some of the slack design here. Movement is absurd, for starters: some players have a disadvantage (worse calipers) with absolutely nothing to compensate. Why? Because. If movement used a point-to-point system, you'd know where you could and couldn't move to without having to measure with the calipers and tip your hand, but this like any sensible idea was swept away by the cuteness of the design. 

I don't know the original, but the version I played was also prone to wild swings - I was ahead for most of the game, and fell behind in the turn before my last due to a gambit by another player. There wasn't anything I could do to recover; he'd just saved up for taking out a hit on someone I needed to live just before the last turn. Ta-da! You lose! 

I guess in theory the clever thing to do would have been to spend some more time shoring up resources to counter the hit, but I don't see it - I'm not sure that my choices would have been radically different had I seen the hit coming. 

There's might be a decent game in here, buried deep, and judging by what I read by lifting bleached_lizard's fixed cards out of their slips, the original was indeed atrocious, with a strange mixture of completely useless and overpowered cards. I don't know if there's any salvaging it, though, and I don't think it's really worth trying.

RATING: 6
In many ways the quintessential Fantasy Flight game: great theme, ambitious ideas, but hobbled execution with some basic design flaws that should have been spotted during even a single playtest.

I like the puzzle aspect, although I would prefer if it let you alter other players' incentives more strongly. I also really like the cardplay, which is where the real meat of the game is. A lot of the cards bring out the characters strengths and weaknesses very effectively, and you need to know a character's deck to play them well. I'm not sure yet whether I want to invest the time to get to that stage, because the presence of the basic flaws mentioned above suggests the game could fall apart on detailed inspection. The cardplay is the only area where the theme really clicks though: sadly, I doesn't feel like I'm solving a murder or conspiracy, but very obviously placing chits on a board. The player really has to do the work to make the theme and mechanics connect.

RATING: 10
This would easily be my favorite game if I could find people willing to play it. I love the interlocking systems, the way the theme is so tightly integrated into play, the balance of gameplay mechanisms (the game is actually like a set of minigames, and the "game" is deciding which one to play when to yield the highest score). The universe is great and the flavor text is the best I've seen. The personal plots and twilight cards are genius, and I LOVE that there are NO DICE. It really feels like a bloated Euro made by a theme-loving American. My only complaint is the game length, which is not too bad once you know what you're doing, but by then, you've scared everyone off. I am working on a solitaire variant that I think has a surprising chance of working.

RATING: N/A
I only read the rules so I cannot put a number on it.

I the worst thing about this game is that there is no investigation at all. You try to bid on the character you want to make guilty or innocent which does not make any sense. In a investigation, you do not make people murderers, you try to find them. 

So that's the thing that bothered me the most. Else, the game seems OK. There is a lot of components, personally I would have tried to cut down in these components.

The twilight system is an interesting mechanic but thematically it makes no sense. You do not constantly shift from good to bad during the weak. You drift toward one side or the other.

As for clues that can be found during the investigation, the player to your right moves the token as if it was a trail. OK, it's simple to implement, but it's not really an investigation trail.

The plot cards story is not a bad idea. Still, I would have prefered a common deck of cards to make sure the story possibilities are almost infinite.

RATING: 9
Solid 8. I'm not sure that the rating could ever go higher for several reasons including:

- This game can be played by 5 players, but I'm not sure I'd ever want to. Time is the major factor here that even though there are a fixed number of rounds, it's still quite long. 3-4 is a great number for this game, and it scales amazingly well.

- There's a ton of fiddle here. I love the system and the theme so I don't mind that much, but the setup is 10x more arduous than the breakdown.

- The learning curve for a game of this length is VERY steep. So steep that they had to have strategy primers in the box (which I'm quite thankful for). Still, it makes new players start at a distinct disadvantage; and given the amount of screwage that you can inflict that could certainly be a factor to discourage repeat plays by newcomers.

It's a great game with quite a number of ways to play, and then there are "mini-games" that have to be managed within each character.  Once we got the kinks out our 3rd play was smooth, fun, involving, challenging, tense, suspenseful, and satisfying.

One final note is that I was surprised at how balanced all the point strategies in the game are; meaning that there is a bonus as well as a foil for every point system in the game. The conspiracy puzzle can benefit early adopters and late comers equally as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is certainly aimed right for my sweet spot. My all-time favorites are BATTLESTAR GALACTICA and Knizia's LORD OF THE RINGS. What I like about both is the brilliant way that they re-tell familiar but highly complex, suspenseful stories through an ingenious combination of text and artwork with game mechanics that are actually _built_ to represent idiosyncratic features of the original stories' plots. 

ANDROID is certainly from the same general family of games, but the all-important difference is that you don't actually know the story beforehand - you're learning it for the first time AS you play the game. And this simply doesn't work, unfortunately. Because one lacks any prior familiarity with the characters, and because the narrative is revealed in such a random, piecemeal fashion, nothing like a narrative structure is ever really discernible.

If it's a failure by these lofty standards, though, then it nonetheless certainly qualifies as a brilliant failure. The rules are enormously complex, but hold together much more intuitively than those of Kevin Wilson's more famous ARKHAM HORROR, and the player is faced with all sorts of interesting tactical choices from start to finish. The art is very good, the flavor text (while a bit cliched occasionally) is always literate and interesting, and the gameworld is a wonderfully original feat of imagination.

RATING: 6.9
Played one game of it.
The setting is alright, the art is ok (though uneven), the game itself is fun, but about two hours too long. The main problems are the extreme fiddliness of the whole thing, the time it takes to play out, and the abundance of stuff to do and to remember - BUT it's much easier than it actually seems on the outset.

Some very interesting mechanisms here, but ultimately it's the kind of game that begs the question - why is this a board game, instead of a video game or RPG? Requires a lot of focus and a lot of good will from the players.

That said, I actually look forward to playing this again.

EDIT 18.11.2012: after a second game, I'm optimistic. There's definitely a pretty good gaming experience here, despite a lot of fidliness and heaps upon heaps of unique rules for almost every card and effect in the game. The kind of game that will definitely be better once played several times with the same group. However, the time it takes is a bit punishing - 4+ hours seem to be the norm, while it should take 3 at maximum to be really enjoyable and keep your attention.

RATING: 6.5
First Impression after on Incomplete 5 player game.  Beware of the BGG recommendation to play this game with less than 5 players, it is right.  The problem with Android is the downtime, which is even worse with 5 players.  It is the typical FFG production, with all the good, the bad, and the ugly that we have come to expect.  The good is the fact that there is an interesting universe, with well defined characters and interesting storylines, good graphics, and overall a very "cinematic" experience that stays with you after the game.  The bad is the rules with all the mini-exceptions, some of the playing aids, some of the mechanics (like the fact that players have to keep track of other players actions/conditions that act as triggers -- for example, you might need to pay attention to when someone's hand has seven cards, etc, many things that can be missed, overlooked, hard to reach/read, slow down gameplay), some of the iconography.  The ugly is the high downtime.  I would like to try this again in three and see whether that helps reduce downtime and focus/streamline/improve some of the mechanics like the investigation.  Otherwise I feel that it has many good ideas (including some bluffing/deception and some interesting mechanics) that are drowned by the size of the game.

RATING: N/A
Android is a board game of murder and conspiracy set in a dystopian future. Detectives travel between the city of New Angeles and moon colony Heinlein chasing down leads, calling in favors, and uncovering the sinister conspiracy beneath it all. The detectives must balance their pursuit of the murderer against their personal lives and their inner demons. Android’s innovative mechanics ensure that no two detectives play alike.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.1
Simply incredible gaming event. Highly thematic crime-drama that feels almost like blade runner. The game is part strategy, part roleplay, part opportunity and plenty of suspicion and player interaction both with the cards and the potential of roleplay. It does take some time to familiarize yourself with the game and to set up and manage everything, but it plays surprisingly smooth and also offers great replayability despite the limited story lines.
Much of the game's joy is the deep amount of story telling the game offers via the plot cards, the crime and the events. 
There is also a tremendous amount of srtategy in the game. Some folks criticize the game as having too many mini-games within the game, but these are simply thematic vehicles of additional VPs. everything in the game is toward victory points, so if you're spending all your time ensuring your character's plot unfolds possitively, that can be just as many points as determinig the guilty person.
Fans of deduction can enjoy this game as well as you can spend a good amount of time trying to figure out who the other players are trying to frame or prove innocent. 

The game is definitely not for everybody, but it can be one of the most rewarding gaming experiences for those who dive into it and emerse themselves in it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10

Although the actual murder investigation is not about deducing who the murderer is (like in Clue), deduction is still required to guess what suspects other players want to be guilty, and this information really affects the outcome of the game.

RATING: 8
Played around 10 or 12 3P games so far.  This game is a beauty.  For people who prefer story, theme, and fun over optimization and cube pushing (nothing wrong with those).  Also, this is not Clue or Sleuth or anything like a deduction game.

RATING: 9
Set in a Blade Runneresque cyber punk universe Android is a wonderfully imaginative journey that plays like a novel.

It is truly unique.  You're a detective working against the other player detectives to prove your guilty hunch.  If you're right your career takes a turn for the better in the form of victory points.  It is NOT a deduction game and an attempt to turn it into one called the "Director's Cut" is just a bastardization that wrecks it.

Your characters have plots....lives that get in the way of their detective work but which if unaddressed will cause them to fall apart costing you major game winning points. In the midst of it all is the conspiracy you work to uncover by playing puzzle pieces -- another unique mechanic.

Android requires patience to play and you need the right group, but there is no other game quite like it.  It's a work of art as much as it is a game.   You'll either love it, or hate it.  I love it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It sticks with you in a different way than other games, and even more so due to the way the setting-think Blade Runner-is handled in the mechanics and design.

RATING: 9
Picked up in trade (for my copy of War on Terror which my playgroup refused to play anymore).

Weird and complex ruleset, but thematically, it doesn't get more appealing - I am a humongous PKD and Heinlein fan, not to mention an oldschool Raymond Chandler and Dashiel Hammett fan.

First play was admittedly slow - just wrapping my head around the mechanics and how everything fits together and interacts, etc. But I was deeply, deeply impressed. This is the immersive storytelling game experience I have always hoped Arkham Horror would be.

To be explicitly clear - it's not really a game in the sense that everything else in my collection is a game. It's a story-management system. I feel completely and totally swallowed up in the setting, I found myself making sacrifices in victory points just because I wanted my character to be... happy, and the events and occurrences actually felt like the events and experiences of a great cyberpunk noir novel.

It's absolutely a work of genius.

-----

edit: 6/13/09 - second play confirms it: played as Floyd - second attempt at plot card "Evil At the Estates." I feel like I just spent the last four hours completely immersed in a rich story, that barely (and only barely) ended happily, and was really more of a commentary on the selfish, brutal, stupidity of humanity, and the likelihood androids will never be human (but for all that, will also never be selfish, brutal, or stupid, and in that sense they will always be both far more and far less than us.)

This isn't a game, it's a work of literature.

The ONLY thing keeping this being a 10 and replacing my favorite game is that the LONG play time, and complex ruleset means there is an extremely limited group of people I will be able to play this with, and it will seldom hit the table. That's ok. It means the times I do play it will be all the more precious.


===========

Some more musings on this game:

In all the debates of Euro vs. Ameritrash, the key difference of design that is most frequently overlooked is whether or not game mechanics are arbitrarily imposed, or thematically meaningful consequences of embedded narrative within game play. This is far more complex than merely saying that Euros have "pasted on themes." 

In my mind, most classic Euros impose arbitrary limitations on players. The limitations to be solved in Euro games are so abstracted that they ultimately amount to pitting the player against [i]the game,[/i] rather than in Ameritrash where players are pitted against [i]each other.[/i] My own proclivities are to be pitted against another player - I frankly could care less about being pitted against game mechanics. Which is why I also find it so damn weird that I like Android.

Why? Because, strangely enough, players ARE pitted against the game itself in Android (and I know many people consider Android to be a clandestine Euro.) HOWEVER: the limitations, rather than being arbitrary and mechanical, are so closely linked to thematic considerations, that each choice feels meaningful in a way that really only happens in theme-heavy Ameritrash. 

This, to me, is one of the reasons why Android is a flawed, but ultimately inspiring, work of literature or art. In literature, characters make decisions not because of the arbitrary machinations of an omnipotent narrator, but because they are so well developed that they cannot but act how they act when they encounter conflict and struggle. Good characters, in literature, make their own decisions. And THIS is what happens during a game of Android...

The game play is frustratingly, wonderfully HUMAN. 

Which itself explains why so many pasty-faced cube-fondlers dislike it.

=================
And this, Android evokes this:
Of all the games I've played, Android seems to come closest to the kind of writing I love. The characters are neither good guys nor bad guys, they struggle as much with themselves as they struggle with the world, and although interesting plot-driven elements exist, in the end, the resolution of the external conflict doesn't matter. Things transcend themselves, and we are left after counting up VP, sitting in the dim light of a dirty cafe in New Angeles, sipping greasy hot black coffee, flicking ash, the smoke twisting from the end of our cigarette like the souls of dead android hookers. We order bad toast, wet eggs, cold bacon, and leaning back look out the window into the dark sparkling city, we see the 'stalk spiraling upward into the sky, not as a triumph, but as a worm, some vast malevolent parasite that has already eaten out the heart of the earth and now, bursting upward, is reaching for the fruit of the moon. And you'd know in that moment, looking at the worm, looking at your reflection in the filty glass of the cafe window, looking at the robot waitress, looking at the headlines on the coffee stained newspaper, looking at everything and nothing, that the worm did it's job well. It's all hollow now. You can solve a case, but you can't save nobody.
-------------------

Slight ratings downgrade. I will always deeply love this game but the pool of people who will play it with me is ever shrinking.

RATING: 7
