=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The best hybridization of a thematic game and a strategy game on the market.

The theme level is immersive - everything from artwork to board design to flavor text works to enmesh you in the Android world.  At the same time, there are numerous routes to victory, as befitting a quality strategy game.  However, none of those routes feels divorced from the overall theme, or tacked on for competitive balance purposes - they are all of the place, and of the time.  The closest description I can think of is the board game equivalent of an open-world, Western-style RPG (think Elder Scrolls).

The core victory path - investigators trying to solve a murder - seems to frustrate some players, because the game doesn't predetermine a villain (a la Clue).  I try to avoid this by describing the scenario as follows: this is a cinematic game.  You are following your character through a rich, immersive world, and deciding which paths to take.  It is a game about discovering, not uncovering.  (Alternately, you're a corrupt antihero who is bending the rules in order to get the suspect you know is guilty.  Your call.)

Are there flaws?  Absolutely.  Don't start the game unless you've got at least four hours to burn, and six or seven may be a safer bet.  The asymmetric player abilities aren't horrifically unbalanced, but some are definitely easier to wield than others.  At times, it just feels like there's *too much*, too many options and paths and possibilities for deviation to just pick a clear strategy and win.

In the end, though, for whatever its flaws, the game is a truly one-of-a-kind experience.  If you approach it with an open mind and a little imagination, it will be unbelievably satisfying.  Just don't forget to break for dinner.

RATING: 8

Other than that, it's a very solid cross-over between the two completely different gaming worlds.

RATING: 3
My first (and only) play was with 5 players - I would not recommend you try this at home! The downtime is excruciating. Top that with annoying "take that" gameplay and you could literally sit for 30 minutes and do absolutely nothing. With fewer players this wouldn't be AS bad, but the repetitive gameplay was enough to be almost thankful that I didn't have to take my turn.

I just don't see the complexity that others find. Confusion... yes, but the gameplay is really rather mundane. The characters are so wildly different that you really need to follow a different path for each. What that means is that the game will play differently each time you have a different character, but in that single game you will do a few things over and over again because if you don't you will not play well. But if the other players think that you're winning and decide to hit you with a few whack-a-mole cards, your gameplan will be all for naught.

I hate to use this statement, but if you like this kind of game, you'll probably like this. It's nicely themed, has plenty of eye candy and takes the better part of a day to play. Please don't ask me to join you, however.

RATING: 7
Android is certainly a niche game within a niche genre.  You have to enjoy experience games first and foremost.  If you are looking for something more than the experience that the game offers via the story and plot developments then there is a very real risk that you will be disappointed.  Beyond that, the game is also a fairly big time-sink.  Even if the playtime gets down to 3 hours or less (which I think is reasonable with 3 experienced players) that can end up being a dealbreaker for a game that emphasizes an experience over control or balance.

Again, I liked the gestalt quite a bit.  The narrative element is substantial and there are some innovative parts to the game play that worked well for me.  I wasn't turned off by the chaos nor the heavy take-that element that the various card effects have, but I can easily see how that could go too far for others.  If I were to make one change it would be to alter some of the point values for the different elements of the game.  Three points for a Jinteki or Haas token seems inconsequential compared to the amount of effort necessary to earn them.  On the other hand, a 30 point swing for the guilty hunch is just colossal. 

In the end, Android is a game I'm happy to own, despite the fact that it may never hit my table again :)

RATING: 3
There are games that the length is not a problem. I have sat down to many, MANY games of Battlestar Galactica knowing that I was in for a 3+ hour game. However, certain games just feel like they drag. This is one of those games.

The other problem with this game is there are WAY too many things going on at once. There are three options for scoring points: 1) Plots, 2) Hunches, 3) Conspiracy. Let me break down the problems here.

1) Plots: The plots can be exceptionally difficult or exceptionally easy, depending on the plot/character and card draws. That said, only a few of these are actually nasty to the point of unplayability.

2) Hunches: With the random draw of evidence, it's actually really, really hard to get your hunches to pay off unless you somehow manage to get every piece of evidence right next to you character.

3) Conspiracy: Probably the most reliable way to earn points/useful stuff. Unfortunately, points earned from here are available to EVERYONE.

These are just my issues from this game after 2 1/2 plays (don't ask). I do enjoy how the cards interact with the game and the excellent flavor, but these don't fix the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Look at Go.  Drop a rock.  There's two other minor rules and THAT'S IT.  Look at this board -- each and every location has at least two pieces of information on it BEFORE specials and chits are introduced.  Most of this information adds unnecessary complexity that actually draws away from the theme, which I assume is the opposite of the designer's intentions.  But after four hours of this white noise, I'm done.  Uncle.  (And the second pass, the one where I knew what I was getting into, was no better.)

That was four incredibly theme-rich, incredibly over-complex, not terribly fun hours that I won't be getting back.  The game tries to be several things at once -- blind-bidding auction game for criminals, Carcassonne tile-play for conspiracy, storytelling for character, and more "take that!" play than most Euro-players will enjoy.  These various elements arrive half-baked in a way that TI3's everything-and-kitchen-sink approach managed to overcome, and as a result the game's theme feels more like "Southland Tales" clusterfuck than the "Blade Runner" dystopian noir it so desperately wants to be.   

The "Southland Tales" parallel is apt, and HiveGod nails it: The game isn't broken, but the magic circle the game creates pops very easily, because the game tries at every opportunity to remind you how very clever it is.  It's an alienating experience, not an immersive one.  And when that's gone, Android loses whatever flavor it's supposed to have and simply becomes a generic Euro with a heavier than usual "take that!" element.  It was a noble experiment and I applaud the effort, but no.

MODIFIED 5/22/15: Wow.  The "Director's Cut" variants really clean up the issues I had with this game: mainly, the verdict system.  I won't play Android without them, and I won't play it with more than 3p.  But under those conditions Android rises from the ashes.

RATING: 6
First of all, this game is LONG. So, do not even consider buying or trying it if you do not have at least 1 hour for rules explanation + 3 hours to play! Do not consider it if the players' knowledge of English is not good enough.

It is a typical setting game, where rules do not play a major role as in German games.
In order to appreciate it, you should take your time to read carefully the plots and the cards' text, otherwise it seems just a sequence of abstract events with poor rules.

The 5 characters are very well detailed with interesting backgrounds. Plots are good and they have a good interaction with cards. In order to be fully appreciated, it requires also a preparation before the game, exactly like a role-playing game. Each characters reflects a different way of playing the game, which becomes evident after a few games.

It is NOT a "murder game": your focus is gathering evidence and not discovering the murderer. Therefore, it seems more a detective game, or a "lawyer" game.

It has some problems with the rules:
- evidence from -5 to +5 make the game too random, and alibi are completely worthless
- being first is worse than being last, and there is nor way to become last
- some puzzle pieces are unbalanced, and it is never convenient to dig through the puzzle.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
To enjoy this, you really need to have the mechanics down pat and to know your card deck - firstly, so that the game moves fast enough and secondly, so that you can wallow in the theme via the copious flavour text on the cards. For 3+ hours. Which means you're likely to struggle a bit through your initial plays, and your liking of the game will reflect your tolerance for theme over downtime. You're working towards three different ways of scoring VPs, and your deck of cards will lean you one or the other. It's an action point game, and you've got 6 APs on each of the 12 turns to move around the board, draw and play cards, or pick up stuff off the board. Most turns you'll want to move around the board's locations to where the evidence chits are, where you can add +ve (guilty) or -ve (innocent) influence points onto a suspect. Each player is trying to get different suspects guilty and innocent for (secret) end-game VPs so that's the main source of competition. Or you could use those evidence chits to add pieces to the conspiracy puzzle, which is literally a jigsaw puzzle, with which you draw lines from the centre out to 8 different possible destinations, each of which change the way VPs score at end-game. How this unfolds affects what becomes more valuable to pick up from the board, and obviously you want to put your 2 bits in to increase the VPs for the stuff you've been collecting. At the same time, you want to collect +ve "baggage" chits, which usually ain't that easy, to ensure that you drive your personal yin/yang plot cards towards +ve VPs. Your opponents will be playing "take that" cards on you to make life tricky, and you'll be doing the same to them. There's a nice mechanism which forces players to alternate between playing good and bad cards, so ganging up seems to add flavour rather than getting out of hand. Most of the game actually seems to be spent studying the cards you have and working out how best to use them (or which to spend San Juan style). The rest of the board-related actions are straight forward. As with all AP type games, getting the best out of your actions, and minimising movement, is key. Adding up all the VPs at end-game can bring surprises, as a large section of points is "unknown" - ie those awarded for guilty and innocent suspects. I'll probably enjoy this more in repeat plays, getting more into the theme, but conversely I'm not sure that the secret suspect and take-that card play mechanisms will sustain a game of this length over the long run.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
After two testplays the feelings are finally clear. It's simple: I like this.

Probably no other game handles the lack of dice more beautifully than this. I didn't even miss some random factors created by dice. The game feels a lot like Arkham Horror (no wonder) and probably that's also why this appeals me.

There is over 300 cards to provide some thematicity to the euro-stylish mechanics. Light twilight cards contribute to the uniqueness of the characters and dark twilight cards make sure that players get kicked and no plan may be a solid one.

One of the best thing in this game is the uniqueness of the characters. They each have personal issues and they really distinct from each other. No player character is even close to being a copy of another. Excellent!

One of the downsides in this game is that scoring is a bit vague. The suspect thing is very clear, but other than that, there are no many ways to score some good points (except Raymond's plots). Sometimes this could lead you to a very difficult position when some suspects are eliminated or just too difficult to prove guilty.

The conspiracy may be tricky. Don't let it grow to every direction without intervention!

The game is best with four players. The dark twilight cards should be played equally to everyone since it's too easy to "kick the leader" in this game. You shouldn't kick the leader since you will have no idea of the leader. We have been mistaken a lot by who really was leading the game. Don't take anyone lightly in this game!

RATING: 8
After having owned the game for more than six months I finally managed to get it on the table. After all the flac this game has recieved I was quite weary, but at least I had to give it a try. After a rather confused and prolonged setup and rules walk through (1h+) the game flowed surprisingly smoothly (of course there where a couple of rules mistakes and a couple of frantic rule book searches) and the game clocked in just above 3h (3players). Not bad.
I really like this game. There is a lot of info to keep track of and it is almost impossible not to miss making an optimal card play every now and then. For the player who wants to feel in full control and who has a hard time accepting mistakes (own or others) and need to make a total analysis of the game situation and possibilites before making a move I can see that this is not the game of choice.
To me Android is an amazingly immersive experience (even more than Arkham horror). I can't tell if it is unbalanced, random or what, but I still like it a lot. If it wasn't for the long playing time and the high learning curve it would get an 8 or even a 9.

RATING: 8.57
I see why a lot of people had an issue with this game, but if you take it for what it is, it is a fascinating game.  A character driven euro... with "character" as in literary characters.  Characters with impulses and motivations and flaws which all express themselves through the mechanics.  The flavor text enhances this, but it sings through the mechanics as well, which is remarkable.  The choices through the plot trees make you really feel like you are steering the character to different outcomes, and the tradeoffs are everywhere.  Balancing the light and dark cards is brilliant as well.  

An achievement, and it is sad the large portion of the gaming community was unable to appreciate it, because I think there is really nothing like it.  And its highly public failure probably discouraged many great ideas at FF for years to come.

Would love to explore this more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.9
This game was surprising and disappointing at the same time. The core game is simple, a murder has been committed and the players represent detectives trying to track them down in a future dystopia, a bit Cluedo, a lot of Bladerunner, and apparently in a long line of quest games coming from Talisman. But this game adds a genuine narrative sense. As you move to investigate different clues they move around, and you and your investigations determine the guilt or innocence not some random predetermination.More importantly you must balance the play of positive cards (played by you) and negative cards (played by you on other players) as you try to balance the devils and demons of your character. In addition characters have personal sub-plots that direct their actions.

Those mechanisms are excellent but on top of them are layered a whole layer of additional sub-games. There is a conspiracy going on and players can unravel it, while there is an economy of 'favours' in various different colours, a series of NPCs for each character, and a set of events which happen at various points. These all feel like the expansion bloat associated with a coin that has had layers of new material added to them but in fact they are all part of the basic game.

So in there is an excellent game. Our group has kind of wondered if you could cut out some of the fat (some of it is only tangentially associated with the core game) and leave a much tighter game, which might play quickly and allow more of the flavour to come through. But as it comes out of the box (or goes back in slowly, you can play a quick euro in the time it takes to set this game up).

RATING: 6
This game should really be better than it is.  Despite a beautiful design, great attention to detail, and a magnificent storyline, the game mechanics are irreparably flawed, your actions and choices are mostly random and don't really involve you in the plot, and for a game that takes 3-4 hours to play, you really don't seem to get to do much, or make many choices.  

With most strategy games taking 2-3 hours at most, a strategy game that can take 4 hours or more just isn't enough to keep most people's attentions, especially considering the incredible amount of down time between turns.  The Director's Cut rules help a lot, but not enough to make the game truly enjoyable, and equal to its peers.

RATING: 3.5
I found the physical components pretty enough, but the pieces didn't really stand out against the busy but attractive board.  The card text was small and difficult to read, with large swaths of card space taken up by flavor text instead of bold, easy to read game text and helpful icons.  In particular, trying to tell how to add baggage to other players plots was practically impossible without just asking the players each time you wanted to know.

Most importantly, though, the game failed to evoke the theme for me and just wasn't that fun a process.  It seemed clear enough that the game itself was going to be highly random, with lots of "take that", so it would be OK to regard it as an experience game...but the experience of it wasn't compelling to me.  I never felt like I was in a cyberpunk or mystery story.  The decisions I had to make weren't tense.  

Just not my thing at all, obviously, certainly not in this time slot.

RATING: 6
I love the theme, the art, and the ambition of this title. There is so much going on, between the murder investigation, the conspiracy (jigsaw) puzzle, your personal choose-your-own-adventure storyline, and all of the cards.... Delving into this game wanting a deep, complex narrative told in a totally unique way is fantastic.

The problem is that this game is really long and complicated to play. It has so many moving parts and different little sub-games that you can play to earn points at the end of the game that it is easily one of the most overwhelming games I've ever tried to learn, let alone introduce to others. Moreover, if the players at the table aren't willing to read out the flavour text of the cards you lose a lot of the story, which is what makes this game worth playing in the first place.

For the right group, in the right mood, with plenty of time on their hands to really dig into the setting, Android has the potential to be fantastic. I really wish I could rate this title higher, but it is rare for all of those elements to fall into place properly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The reviews -- both good and bad -- are accurate.  Our first game (3 players) took one hour to teach and four hours to play.  That was after I studied the rules for a week and created pages and pages of player aids.  The second "week" went much better than the "first," after we were more used to the play.

The theme is as much or as little as you make of it.  It can easily devolve into a series of sterile mechanics if you let it; the theme is in your imagination, participation, and frame of mind.

There is a LOT to keep track of at any time in the game, especially the various triggers for your possible actions.  Once we got the hang of the cards, we had a lot of fun playing "take that."

My first play was not as fun as I had hoped, but I plan to play again, with the expectation that several sessions will be necessary to fully appreciate this game.

RATING: 6
The reviews are pretty accurate for the game, but I take them in a *good* way. This is film noir game, in which the players are writing separate stories for the characters. Much like film noir, the murder is unimportant. It's the character's personal struggles that are center of the story. 

In our first game, we *were* able to play with five players. I moderated and one player had played before. We stopped after four hours and after the first week of the game. But, by then, we accomplished quite a bit: The conspiracy puzzle was pretty much filled, we resolved our plots, we had a guilty suspect, and even Vinnie had been killed off!

I think this game had some unfortunate expectations, and it's not as accessible as other FFG games. But if you enjoy film noir -- and, I mean, *really* enjoy it -- play a game of Android.

RATING: 7
Over the top FFG production values, combined with bargain basement prices mean that I would be happy for this game to sit on my shelves virtually unplayed and unlikely to be played - which is essentially the situation. I can see this going down very well with the right group of players, although the necessity to learn a bunch of odd mechanics is a necessary barrier, as according to comments here on the geek is the 'fit up a suspect' rather than 'solve a mystery' gameplay - not a problem for me personally:it seems to fit in well with the dystopian sf background, and makes for an interesting and very gamey mechanic - always useful in a board game. 
Edit after a proper 4 player game: Plays extremely well and fits my expectations above. For me the biggest problems were - 
1) having to go through the rule book to give a rules explanation owing to not having 'revised' properly before play - with set up this took about an hour and greatly annoyed Mrs Schkff. Solution: prepare properly
 2)all the flavour text being a bit messy - we opted not to go through the procedure of everybody reading out their text so it was optional for players if they wanted to read their own flavour text or not. The game manages to flow on reasonably well almost completely ignoring the text, but some sense of the story is lost a bit here. As with the rules explanation, this problem was really just down to our own approach to the game, and did not spoil play. Solution: either want to read the text, or be happy ignoring it; not a particularly big deal.
3) Mrs Schkff being impatient about how long I apparently took on my turns, whilst wanting to stop and check if she was able to play a dark card on every player after what seemed like every single move. Grr. Solution: Do not play Android with Mrs Schkff.
4) Some annoying vagueness about how to use the day marker track, and a number of questions about the conspiracy board. Solution: read the rules properly.
All these problems aside, this was an intense and for three of us very enjoyable game, which in the end could have gone either way - this with a great variety in the strategy and approaches each player took. The whole evidence planting mechanic is pure genius, far more interesting than any deduction game could possibly be. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Don't see what the big problem is, rules though complicated are more intuitive and less fiddly than arkham (game play is actually quite similar when you get down to it), with a more personal and engaging story element. One of a kind really, like being in a novel and much underated.

RATING: 8
Players are investigators that try to solve a crime at a turning point of their lives in a [i]Blade Runner[/i] setting.  They get points for discovering the criminal and the conspiracy behind his/her behaviour, for defending an accused inocent and for resolving their personal affairs in a good way. In NYC (Dec 2011)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[thread=364669][/thread]

An [i]experience[/i] that uses a game to make you care about an ambitious setting with its flawed cast of characters.  The flavor text, so drab and puerile in novel form, grips me in the context of a game.

Old-school Ameritrash sometimes tips the experience toward 1 player, with the other player serving as an 'AI' (e.g. Space Hulk). This one takes it a step further, with you working on the narrative of your detective, while simultaneously playing God for the other 2 players, helping with upkeep and putting down 'smart' events at the right time. And it's other 2, because 3 is way way optimal (like old-school Ameritrash, the downtime gets too much with each player having a bunch of actions to optimize and spend at once).

The engine that drives the game is the light/dark resource duality, and needing to manage it to properly time your card-play. It's fun, and forces you to strike a balance between helping yourself on your turn, versus screwing others on their turns (and there's a ton of direct screwage; view of it as teaching your opponents life lessons!); drawing cards and picking things up along the way, versus spending them to lower card costs. Reminiscent of Mammoth Hunters and Lords of the Rings TCG (hey, it's called twilight too!), but the engine is far more integrated into the theme than in either of those.

Four main things to do:
1. Place evidence on suspects to solve the crime and prove your guilty hunch (hidden objectives, hidden majority system, with plenty of bluffing and negotiation) for a ton of end-game VPs.
2. Work on the conspiracy puzzle, building a cute communal jigsaw puzzle to link factions to the murder, and raise the VP values of certain resources.
3. Accumulate good baggage to resolve your narrative arc favorably, and have your happy endings.
4. Trade between resources via board locations.

Thematic integration on all levels. Every innovative mechanism has a place in the theme, and is thoughtfully blended together. The flavor text adds so much color that you have to consciously stop reading them so you'll actually have a shot at finishing the game.

The only real drawback is downtime, since you take your actions all at once, and have lots of ways to optimize, but pick suitable opponents and you're fine. Plus I'm having a ball on others' turns reading flavor text, thinking up my next day, and looking out for opportunities to play dark cards.

Definite perfect 5 on design, and while I'm not sure this one has legs beyond the 20 or so plays the cards are supposed to provide (got abandoned expansion-wise due to the mass of wusses that misunderstands this game), this will be a 4 or 5 on want-to-play until further notice.

bleached_lizard develops thoughtful variants for several FFG games, and his [filepage=53229][/filepage] thoughtfully points out and tries to fix thematic inconsistencies. Will explore when I tire of the game.

5+4

RATING: 9.5
Currently my highest rated thematic behemoth (and one of 2 I tolerate). Simply put, while overhead is crazy and investment borderline tolerable, the payoff is worth it. To me anyhow. 

It's a a novel, or is it 5 novels about 5 detective on the brink of emotional, professional and general collapse in a nicely coloured cyberpunk Los Angeles of future. Not that they've noticed, they're in a noir story. And it's rough. These novels are cut into pieces, glued to cards, shuffled, a lot of thematic, semi thematic and kinda thematic mechanisms added and voila. 

Going in you have to know:
- 3 player only. (you don't have a week, do you?)
- This is a story driven game.  There will be take that effects that can make you lose half a game of progress in this or that, so it's better to embrace the game for the story and don't get too upset about the winning part. It's a good idea to prioritise personal quest above other in game goals. 
- If you're good the game takes only an hour to teach. Yeah - there's a teaching script in Android's bgg forums.
- universal head players aids and rules summary. Print it. 
- Oh yeah, probably worth saying, you're not out there to solve a murder, you're there to solve your own miserable experience by framing a murder on someone. Future is bleak. 

The whole thing is huge and overwhelming and kinda slow, but the good bit is that there are short term, middle term and long term goals and lists of things to do, which helps in basic orienteering. But given time and proper company to delve into the atmosphere of the game (put some 1990s drum and bass on) it's as immersive as these kind of games can get. 

I think I mostly enjoyed soloing it as you can really delve into the story part and atmosphere. But yeah, it is on the brink of collapsing under its weight and probably has too many things going on. And you'd probably need a second (robotic) brain to keep track of rules and strategize while your mind can focus on the narrative.

RATING: 7
[i]75% Atmosphere, 25% Game... the Game Bit Isn't Super Strong, but it is Unique and Endearing![/i]

Will only play with 2-3 players.

Heard this was a love it or hate it game -- can't say I love it yet, but I [i]certainly[/i] do not hate it!

It's refreshing to see a game designed with such care, effort, and LOVE. This game is the craft of a man who wanted to make something significant -- when it comes to personality and flavor, it puts 90% of my shelf to shame.

[b]Cons:[/b] The downtime is significant and the game is a little messy... you probably wouldn't want to play this with any more than 3 players, as the playtime would stretch too far and the focus and interaction spread too thin. But what this game has going for it is its uniqueness. It's not being "outdone" by any other game I've played.

I suspect that the finite stories though are what give this game its legs for me. I was not as charmed by the mechanics as I was by the story. So not sure I will have a long-term relationship with this one, and conservative rating anticipates that

RATING: 3
Perhaps this year's biggest disappointment.  For all the cool elements to the design, there was just too much going in without any reward.  I feel like there are a number of cool and innovative mechanics that are just there for the sake of innovation, rather than leading to a more fulfilling game.  Piling stuff on with a "wouldn't it be cool if we added this" mentality.  

The caliper car travel and the awkwardly abstract twilight track are the best examples.  While they are fun in concept, ease and speed of play, and forward planning, all fall prey to unfortunately appropriate min-maxing.  I think it is a poor design to take all the down time from action points in Java or Tikal, and then make every action point spent tough to plan for or map out.  Oof.  

Planning was the biggest issue that I had.  There are too many "take that" cards. They are constantly foiling the extreme level of overhead that is required for decent play. Not infrequently, you will take your time and energy planning for the next turn on other players turns, but find it is all for naught. Often everything is changed from the chaos of people taking actions & playing cards. Not such a big deal.  You get the point where you realize that this is a tactical game. But, the expect people to give up planning when it isn't their turn.  

So, you recognize that you have to have a loose plan.  Fine.
Then the whammy cards come out. You aren't allowed to take your turn or you lose the work of your last few turns.  They operate to such an extreme that everything you do can be negated.  Causing someone to lose their turn is the least fun thing that you can design into a game. 

Still, you should attempt to plan your turn despite all this.  I just don't enjoy all the overhead when all of it goes out the window with crazy cards.

I felt that what you do on your turn was pretty repetitive with no real reward other than accumulating specific chits.  Story cards drive what you try to collect, so most of the time you are doing the same actions over and over again because that is what your character is supposed to do.  So you spend the game grinding to accumulate baggage to counter other player's attacks, or money or whatever a character specifically needs.

I feel that everything in this game is creating friction.  It is so hard to do anything well.  This isn't a Wallace thing where you want to four things but you can only do two.  You may not be able to do what you need to, but you still have to puzzle everything out.  

I love that you are trying to plant evidence on players that you don't like.  It is cool in concept that you get happy every time you try to do something cruel to another player, but all of it just doesn't add up.

This game is looooong.  Way to long for whatever could be the reward.  The story and color are great, I'd actually like to see more of this world, but not here.  I'd rather just read all the cards and get the instant gratification rather than suffer through the tedium to flip the page in the choose your own adventure.

Four hours in, I finally lost my moral check on this and quit.  I didn't give anyone the chance for The Bone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
It's just too dang long. 12 turns x 3 players x 6 minutes per turn + 24 minutes for overhead = 4 hours. Playing with 4 or 5 players isn't a realistic option. 6 minutes per turn isn't unreasonable. You usually have 6 or 7 "action points", and while some things cost 2 or more, most actions only cost 1. You've got to move tokens around and read cards that you draw, and other players can interrupt your turn with negative card plays. So, you can't really shave down that 6 minutes per turn number any.

As theme-rich as the game is, it's not "really" a murder mystery. I've compared the scoring mechanic to Reef Encounter and Arkadia where you're trying to influence the end-game values of different scoring markers that you collect during the game. There's also one scoring rule out of left field that was dropped in straight from an abstract strategy game and makes no sense thematically.

I found the management of the personal plots to be the most interesting part of the game. Ironically, this is thematically the distraction from the objectives of solving the murder and uncovering the conspiracy. However, as my win in the last game shows, "solving" the murder gives you an almost insurmountable 15-20 point lead, so I fear that any future plays would degenerate with even more effort placed on influencing the murderer.

The theme and the personal plots were fun, but I can't see myself investing the time to play it again.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game seems very polarizing, but as a Blade Runner-esque game along the lines of Arkham Horror -  except competitive - I couldn't resist when available at basically half price. Will probably be playing it in a week to evaluate.

UPDATE: After one play, I'm already feeling it. This is a game I'm going to always look forward to playing. I've begun to equate it to Yes' album Tales from Topographic Oceans: A work of great achievement and by NO means for everyone, but that's a whole other post.

At its core, a very Euroesque multiple paths to victory point scoring system with about 12 layers of theme added. It really does feel like a kind of competitive Arkham Horror - but rather than the board randomly throwing obstacles at you to deal with, the other players create the problems at times of their choosing to best hinder your efforts. It's a great system of introducing "screw your neighbor" rules that any rational person cannot take personally - because EVERYONE needs to play that way. The currency you need to play good events for yourself is gathered by playing bad events on others, and vice versa. A very elegant (yes, I used the word elegant with Android) form of direct conflict.

You could really explore the five detectives' strength and weeknesses - as they play so differently, honing this familiarity to produce the outcomes you desire. Hopefully I'll get the chance to play often enough to do so.

Played with 2 players which works very nicely, just make sure you use 4 suspects. Would like to try 3 players, but would be hesitant to play with more than that.

Basically, one heck of a $20 buy.

update 2: Still really digging it after numerous plays of 2-players up to 5. 5 is too many, but still enjoyed the game.

RATING: 6.5
Very unique and interesting game. Definitely has a lot of innovative mechanisms and ideas, but a lot of them seem random and not integrated together. The branching story aspect is really awesome, and the game exudes theme. The pinning a suspect is neat too, although a bit random; still a decent design choice. Also, the "take that" cards and "boost yourself" cards seem to work fairly well with a whole balancing act. However, the whole conspiracy puzzle and moving around the board with a ruler feels really out of place. 

There are a few thematic disconnects, but the biggest one is the conspiracy puzzle by far. Not really sure how you uncover some conspiracy between political favors and street favors and negative story endings and whatever. Also, there are some weird design decisions in the game. Like some plot bonuses being great for your first ending plot (every future plot gets 1 good baggage at the start), but would be completely useless if it was your second plot.

Characters are very different and all feel both unique and alive. You really want to play out their story and see where it goes. Unfortunately, it is layered behind so much stuff that it is hard to fully enjoy.

Ultimately, the game is fun and definitely interesting, but it could use a major re-design and overhaul. Some aspects just simply don't work like the huge travel times it takes to get from the Moon to Earth. Plus, each player really should know what Dark cards can be played on them since some of them are devastatingly strong.

Still, it is a game I'd suggest people who enjoy thematic games with lots of narrative definitely check out at least once. Just go into it knowing that the game is more about the story you tell than the actual individual pieces of the game.

RATING: 8
Only played once so far, but the first impression was good.
But don't let yourself be fooled - there is no real murder case to solve here like in Clue(do) or other games like this.
Actually, what you do is to try to frame your prefered suspect for murder! ;-)
There are different murder cases to play, with different event card decks. The playable detectives all have their "life-stories" to go through - to the bitter or the good end.
Nothing in this "murder mystery" game is fixed and needs to be unconvered - instead, each player DESIGNS (or tries to, anyway) HIS murder case. You try to frame your prefered suspect, you create a conspiracy theory, you try to bring your life episodes to a good end, and you try to bring those of other detectives to a bitter one.
Once you get your head around this and the game's complex rules, I assume this can be a rewarding experience.
Don't expect to get everything right in your first game - that will just teach you how to play the game, show you what you have to remember next time, and understand what you did wrong.
I'm now waiting for my next chance to play this and hopefully I'll have the full Android-experience then.

Update 2012-Sep-03: Had my second game yesterday and a lot of fun. Next time, though, I think I will try a little variant with the conspiracy puzzle, and only award +4VP each time a player is the first to establish a link to one of the 8 outer groups, rather than awarding them for completing a row of the puzzle.

Update 2013-April-07: We played with the normal conspiracy puzzle rules, and it was fine. Limiting these points would just limit the chances of some players to score points.

RATING: 5.5
No other game polarizes me as much as Android, at least after my first kick at the can.  During the first "day," I was seriously contemplating where among my favourite games this would lie: top 10, top 5, even #1?  I was loving it, or at least I was loving what I thought it was going to be.  After a few hours, though, I was deflated.  There is so much potential but too many abstract design decisions undermine it, taking what should be a richly thematic experience into a dry and kind of awkward set of procedures.  The flavour text may be there (too much, even -- I can't imagine how long a game would take if the players actually read it all out loud) but the mechanics obstruct the game's narrative.  

Probably the biggest blunder in the design is that the winner is decided by victory points.  The fact that this game is decided any way other than solving the murder/conspiracy is ridiculous.  And all the game's failings stem from this as players spend their time analyzing efficient avenues to accumulate victory points rather than, you know, TRYING TO SOLVE THE F'N MURDER!

Hopefully the story will shine through once the gameplay becomes more familiar, but I'm skeptical.  As it stands, Android is one of my bigger gaming disappointments.

UPDATE: I've tried it a second time, years later, and it still felt overly procedural. I've given up on it for good.

But I'm glad that FFG has used the setting in other games (Infiltration, Netrunner), because it is an eye-catching one.

RATING: 7
About [thing=39339][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=39339][/thing] is a highly thematic game set in the world of tomorrow - the dread future, a dystopian place, sure, but not a wasteland or zombie infested. In this future, megacorporations run things and there are huge conspiracies happening. And there are laws. Mostly the same we have today.

Players will be detectives, trying to solve a murder case, while they also have to deal with their personal lives and their inner demons. Now, the suspect is different for each player, and during play, one of your tasks is to collect enough evidences to put [i]your[/i] suspect in jail. This is a neat thing. [thing=39339][/thing] isn't a deduction game, isn't [thing=1294][/thing], or [thing=2511][/thing] or [thing=594][/thing]. No, in [thing=39339][/thing] the one who did it is the one players say did it.

To do this, players travel between the city of New Angeles and Heinlein, a moon colony, going after clues, testimonies and leads, gaining favors, using those favors to receive help, access and other things.

There is also a inner battle between light and darkness, as using cards to help herself makes the players go towards the dark, and using cards to hinder other players will lead to light. Yes. Not what you would excect, right?

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
At the start of the game a scenario for the whole play is chosen or randomly drawn. Then every player receives two cards: one Guilty and one Innocent card, each one showing one of the suspects. One of the tasks is to use evidences those people fit the description received. Also, players take, at random, of personal objective to pursue in the first week, then a second goal for the second week.

Players have time on their turn - usually 6. Each hour is used doing one of these things:
a) Going to a new location - this is done by using a pretty neat "car range" ruler, which shows how far can the detective's card can reach and one hour;
b) Follow a Lead - either directly regarding a suspect or unravelling the conspiracy;
c) Draw/Discard a card;
d) Play a Light card;
e) Get a jump on the case;
f) Use the ability of a location (this varies in time used).

A player uses all her hours before the turns passes to the next player. After all the player had a turn, the timer advances (usually triggering an Event). After 6 rounds, the "week" ends. After two weeks, the game is over.

Evidences are revealed and the suspect with the highest sum of evidence tokens will be the culprit. The one with the lowest sum, will be the innocent. Players will score accordingly. Also, several things are worth points (fulfilled personal goals, favors, etc) and these can be worth even more due to the conspiracy. The winner will be the person with the most points.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
Several. Which place to visit, which lead to follow, if advance in the conspiracy, or put evidence in the suspects (and in which suspect), if use a location ability, to deal with the light and dark in order to make use of good (for the player) or bad (for others) cards, how to handle the personal goals and the task of investigating the murder.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Uniqueness: [thing=39339][/thing] isn't like any other game I have played, even if the actions aren't unique, the whole is;
- Knee deep in theme;
- The sci-fi setting works pretty well;
- Lots of ways to gain points - framing the proper suspect isn't a requirement to win (but helps a lot);
- Several options to do in a turn: there is always something good to do.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Downtime: is [b]huge[/b]. Playing with 4 or 5 would be something I wouldn't advise to an enemy, as I'm against torture;
- Lots of fiddly rules make teaching the game a chore and remembering everything a challenge;
- Killing a suspect is something I find dreadful in the way it is handled. Though this doesn't eliminate a player, it is a enormous step back. Hits should only take evidence tokens out, as this would also make valuable to hit other suspects when someone already has 1 or 2 hits;
- Limited player interaction;
- The take that cards. They work well inside the theme, and are the biggest part of interaction between players. I'm not against them, but the way they work is, to me, weird. Normally higher value cards do the most harm, and also help most the player using it (as she gains light points to use good cards in her own turn), and for me it should work differently: cards take do most harm should have low value, helping almost nothing for you to gain light points, but hindering the other players more; while high value would do little harm, but would be worth more lights points. Yes, it would probably required a maximum of 1 card used per player for it to work, but I'm sure it would;
- There are several bad cards that lower the time available in the turn of other players. Add this to the downtime and you have a recipe for resentment.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like being a mix of Rick Deckard ([i]Blade Runner[/i]) with Del Spooner ([i]I, Robot[/i]) and a nudge of John Murdoch ([i]Dark City[/i]). There are no true good guys - everyone is at least half in the dark. There are very bad people, though. The future is bleak, but not without hope: there are room for hapiness, for success, for achievement, even beyond the Laws.

Anyway, [thing=39339][/thing] is a dense game, with lots of rules, long, fiddly and hugely thematic. It is weird, quirky and beautiful in its own ways. Plays will leave memories, will be talked about. Definitely not a game for everyone, as there is a big downtime, "take that" between players, opaqueness in strategy as there is a good deal in the hands of random.

[thing=39339][/thing] is like its setting: not easy to get by, but, play your chips right, and you might just do well enough. It is far from perfect, still, it is hard to look away, with all the lights, colors, life, schemes, all surrounded in a dark and mysterious atmosphere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Android is a board game of murder and conspiracy set in a dystopian future. Detectives travel between the city of New Angeles and moon colony Heinlein chasing down leads, calling in favors, and uncovering the sinister conspiracy beneath it all. The detectives must balance their pursuit of the murderer against their personal lives and their inner demons. Android’s innovative mechanics ensure that no two detectives play alike. Will you play as Louis Blaine, the crooked cop tormented by guilt and loss? Or will you take the role of Caprice Nisei, the psychic clone who struggles to retain her sanity while proving that she’s as human as anyone else? Whoever you choose to play, you’ve got just two weeks to solve the murder, uncover the conspiracy, and face your personal demons.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Android is an investigation game where the answer is never truly known even when resolved.  This makes it more gritty and real to life than typical deduction games.  Android has a deceptive amount of interaction and intrigue.  It may be the most underrated game on bgg due to player expectation rather than implementation.  Length, including scale up with more players, is a bit of an issue.  I would pin Android as a dedicated 3 player game.  Overall excellent but the player number limitation prevents a higher score.

RATING: 8

RATING: 4
Really wanted to like this game. Artwork and theme are amazing, but it felt like too much personal storytelling was required to become invested in what was happening. And the amount of emphasis on story diminished repeated plays for me, as you start to see the same cards again (there aren't that many).

The (large number of) different mechanics did not feel like they meshed particularly well, and made setup, learning, and teaching the game extremely long. Despite the number of mechanics, the actual gameplay felt restrictive; the dirty cop brawler character can't actually fight in any meaningful way, etc.

There is also a very high amount of "take that" for such a long game. Without thorough knowledge of each character's cards (which works against the emergent storytelling aspect), you could be blindsided and destroyed by certain cards without any way to compensate.

I really like the world and would love to see a version that was shorter, simpler, and slightly more thematic in its gameplay.

Comments based on 3 plays with 2-3 players. Unlikely to play again.

RATING: 8
Not for everyone.  Just because you are a fan of FFG games does not mean this will fit in well with your collection.

This one can be very challenging to teach.  You're better off if you can find a couple of people who really want to play it who will take the time to read the rules on their own prior to your first game.

This is not a deduction game.  There is no predetermined murderer.  You are playing an investigator trying to build a case against the suspect you believe is guilty.

The game can be cruel.  Actually, it is the other players, acting as the hand of fate, can be cruel.  However, this is a necessary mechanic of the game, as the main way to play your own good cards on yourself is by using other players' bad cards on them.

This is one that I like, and fortunately, is gaining popularity with my gaming group.

RATING: 8
Lavish, ingenious, and original - Tales from the Arabian Nights as a tile-laying area-influence game.  

While the actual murder-mystery part of the game is weirdly irrelevant, it really doesn't matter.  The characters' plotlines are really interesting and the struggle over their outcome is compelling.  

Despite the apparent complexity, the game flows smoothly once the mechanics all click.  It's hard to pay much attention to what's on the cards until that happens - there's an awful lot to get sorted out in your head - but once it does, the atmosphere really works.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Rating this game low was hard for me because of the wonderfully beautiful artwork story.  If you want your game nights to resemble a movie more than a cooperative game, then this may be just the game for you, though.

Each player controls a character that has multiple plot lines.  Each plot line takes a week in the game time, and has 4 possible outcomes, based upon the circumstances at 2 other points during the week.  I think this is an amazing concept.

Moving around the board felt much like playing Pac-man.  You seek out the trails that have the densest packing of dots, and often try to move in one general direction for a nice powerup.  Since all other players must watch you make your path each turn, this means that turns take a very long time, yet they are rarely strategic.

I really want to like the game, but I can't.

RATING: 7
Beautiful in both concept and theme, but deeply flawed, unfortunately.  The story here is amazingly rich, with all the personal goals and differing story the real reason to play this game.  But, then a 4+ hour game gets in the way of the narrative, and what inertia the game has dies in a riddle of interrupts, slowdowns, and just plain wasted actions.  The writing, although corny, works well to set the mood. Each of the characters feels a lot different.  I don't know of any other boardgame that builds a story this strong for its characters.

In some ways, Android feels like it is two different games.  One is a story-driven card interaction game, with players playing light and dark cards to explore a world.  The other is a rather dry adventuring experience with resource collecting and hidden allocation.  Either on its own would be good, but the two together slow each other down to an unacceptable degree.

RATING: 6
I typically love Fantasy Flight's enormous productions, those games where you get assigned homework in order to be prepared to play the game (which is a true story). In that sense, Android did not disappoint: it was deep, thematic, and narrative. I loved that you had good cards for your character and bad card for the other characters. However, there was so much going on and so much of that was out of your control. I enjoyed my game and would be hesitantly interested in playing again, but I hesitate because it was a big time investment for a game where I'm not sure I had enough control.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Android is incredibly exotic - unlike anything I've ever played - so we're lucky that it exists at all.   It's too detailed, earnest, and unusual to be a mainstream hit, so hats off to FFG for letting it all hang out.  You should definitely give it a whirl, regardless of what anyone else says or thinks.  

Admiration aside, based on my 10 games' worth of experience, there's one crippling flaw that rides my saddle the wrong way.

Like Dungeon Lords, Android takes a serious misstep in its bottom-line reliance on victory points.  It's not implausible, for example, that a player might skip the theme entirely and mindlessly gun for high-point favors just for the sake of winning.  Of all the possible ways for the game to be decided, favor-gathering is certainly the most mechanical and least satisfying.  If the game didn't have victory points at all, or if certain point categories were eliminated or minimized, I'd be more tempted to pull this out regularly.

Specific aspects of the game don't scale well, either, such as the hit-to-kill threshold for suspects.  It's much more difficult for suspects to accumulate three hits in a three-player game than in a five-player game - but I'm not going to add two players and two additional hours just to balance out the suspect-killing probabilities.

But honestly, these drawbacks aren't sufficient cause for me to turn up my nose at this piece of classic gaming literature.  Android's dash into futuristic grit and criminal despair is a refreshing change of pace, and it certainly deserves at least a half-point ratings bump for Innovations in Gaming.

RATING: 2.5
A lot of new concepts, very good artwork, but all resulting in a terrible game. Too confusing to be played by casual gamers, too long to be replayed enough to discover the strategies of each character (who are apparently unique to each other).

For a game called Android, unless a player picks a specific character, there is no Android. For a game about solving a murder case, winning the game doesn't specifically rely on getting the murder correct. Gameplay feels too much like placing blame on suspects than over uncovering evidence - relying heavily on player imagination to see beyond the system to become thematically accurate.

A good inspiration which led to developments in other games by FF, but on its own its a failed product that I would recommend no one buy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a pinnacle of boardgame originality. They could easily have made this a bleak whodunnit detective game and be done with it, but instead the designer drank a huge cup of inspiration lemonade and made a futuristic film noir character branched story development adventure game where you push into existence a conspiracy and frame a murderer because you already believe you know who the murderer is, you just need proof. All this while your character is dealing and struggling with his/her own life problems.  

However this is a difficult game to get an audience because it's unusual. And things that dare to do new things are often skipped for the next run-of-the-mill copy of something that has already been done a thousand times. It doesn't help that the game on top of its unusual gameplay is loaded with a confetti heap of tokens. Yet it is harder to explain the game than it is to play it. Once you get going, you roll with the flow. And if I wasn't clear about it yet, this game is an art of work, a refreshing fountain of originality.

The one negative I could tell is that it takes a while if you play with slow players and people that overthink it, because you plan your whole day during your turn. 

The gameplay is diverse. An overview at what you'll be doing : managing your given time, picking up evidence all over the place, linking conspiracy paths to score more vp's and bonuses, trying to deal with the suspects if you can, managing your characters life, dealing out troubles to your opponents, getting hit back with karma, cruising around in a car, taking a space elevator, mapping the useful locations for your strategy, bluffing with your hunch, finding out the story behind your character and accomplishing a good end to your story.

RATING: 9
Very impressed after an initial play - great concept, wonderfully executed theme with good writing and lots of interesting mechanics going on. It'll take a lot of plays to get the full measure of this game.

RATING: 7
3 players seems to be the sweet spot for this game.  It is dense and should only be played with a group that is interested in getting into the theme (which oozes from every mechanic).  It's definitely not a game for every games day, but when the mood is right, it's great.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I really enjoy this game, and my enjoyment is likely due to my interests in role-playing games, dystopian stories, and heady/complex games (though the intuitive system within Android isn’t as complex as it might initially appear.  I find it to be very organic.)  I agree that you should read the narrative excerpts on the cards as you play the game; they add that extra spice that brings the game to life much more than treating it as simple flavor text.  

There are several layers to the game and managing them is a beautiful challenge.  How well is your character getting on in his/her personal life?  Are you able to capitalize on the conspiracy?  That next piece of evidence is in a seedy part of town, but you’re fairly certain that you can point a significantly damning finger at your suspect with it; however, are any of the other players holding your Dark Cards and are they dark shifted enough to possibly do some harm to you if you step into that location? 

As for complaints with the game: the production quality of several of the components has been found lacking.  The box is somewhat soft, and I’m concerned it won’t hold up through the years.  Also, the layered cardstock on many of the tokens is also soft and under threat of fraying.  None of my other FFG games have this issue, so perhaps my copy of Android is an unfortunate case.  For the most part, the artwork in the game is really good, but the art of Vinnie the Strangler is annoyingly skewed, and the unnecessary cleavage of Eve 5VA3TC is sadly objectifying. 

My closing thoughts on the game:  It’s a beautifully designed game of theme and narrative, and I would classify it as one of my favorites.

But, once get understood the play flows well and everything stays very well to each other.

RATING: 6
Like nothing you've played before.  Visually it certainly has a wow factor, and a strong story element runs deeply through the game, but playing with any more than 3 means a PAINFUL amount of downtime.
Many have an issue with the way the guilty are determined - if you go into this expecting Cluedo or Mystery of the Abbey, you WILL be disappointed.  It's neither of these games, nor does it try to be.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Checked the Director's Cut variants. After second reading the Murder and the Twilight -variants look interesting. Maybe also the Conspiracy. With standard rules the player who had guilty and innocent hunches right was the winner in those games I've played, and the huge number of dismissed evidence seemed to affect the outcome too much. But maybe the other players weren't efficient enough to collect VP from alternative sources...
